Restrictive vs Complete Relations
Commentators explain the division of nisab into restrictive and complete, with subcategories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between restrictive and complete relations and identify their subtypes in Arabic phrases.

Core Idea
Every relation is either a complete utterance or a restriction that qualifies a term.

In logic, before analyzing propositions, we must understand the basic unit of relation (nisba). This lesson breaks down the two types of relations: those that form a complete statement and those that merely qualify a term. The passage from Tadhheeb, along with commentaries by al-Dasuqi and al-'Attar, clarifies this fundamental division and resolves a common confusion about examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories of nisba
The matn states that nisab are either restrictive (taqyidiyya) or complete (tamma). Note that 'restrictive' means the relation does not form a complete sentence; 'complete' means it does.
The key test: can you remain silent after uttering it and feel the utterance is complete?
2
Understand the criterion: proper to remain silent
Al-Dasuqi explains that a restrictive relation is one where it is not proper to remain silent (لا يحسن السكوت عليها). In contrast, a complete relation is one where it is proper to remain silent (يحسن السكوت عليها).
This criterion is operational: try pausing after the phrase; if it feels unfinished, it's restrictive.
3
Subdivide restrictive relations
Restrictive relations are of two types: descriptive (tawsifiyya), where the second term describes the first, e.g., 'the speaking animal' (الحيوان الناطق); and additive (idafiyya), where the second term is in the construct state, e.g., 'the servant of Zayd' (غلام زيد).
In both cases, the second term restricts or specifies the first.
4
Recognize the commentator's correction
Al-Dasuqi notes that the matn's examples 'the speaking animal' and 'the servant of Zayd' might be misread as complete propositions with a hidden predicate. He clarifies that they are intended only as examples of the restrictive relation itself, not full statements.
This illustrates the importance of focusing on the relation alone, not the surrounding context.
5
Examine complete relations
Complete relations are subdivided into non-informative (like commands: 'strike!') and informative (like declarative sentences, e.g., 'Zayd stood' when you are uncertain). Al-'Attar adds that forms of composition (insha) like commands are treated based on their conventional meaning, not on any underlying informative relation.
Complete relations can be either informative (khabariyya) or non-informative (insha'iyya).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إمَّا تَقْيِيدِيَّةٍ: كَالْحَيَوَانِ النَّاطِقِ، وَغُلَامِ زَيْدٍ. وَإِمَّا تَامَّةٍ: غَيْرَ خَبَرِيَّةٍ كَاضْرِبْ، أَوْ خَبَرِيَّةً مَشْكُوكَةً. قَالَ الدَّسُوقِيُّ: (قَوْلُهُ: إِمَّا تَقْيِيدِيَّةٌ) هِيَ النِّسْبَةُ الَّتِي لَا يَحْسُنُ السُّكُوتُ عَلَيْهَا، وَيَكُونُ أَحَدُ الْأَمْرَيْنِ فِيهَا، وَهُوَ الثَّانِي قَيْدًا لِلْأَوَّلِ، وَهِيَ قِسْمَانِ: تَوْصِيفِيَّةٌ: وَهِيَ الَّتِي يَكُونُ الثَّانِي فِيهَا وَصْفًا لِلْأَوَّلِ، كَالنِّسْبَةِ فِي الْحَيَوَانِ النَّاطِقِ، وَإِضَافِيَّةٌ: وَهِيَ الَّتِي يَكُونُ الثَّانِي فِيهَا مُضَافًا إِلَيْهِ، كَالنِّسْبَةِ فِي غُلَامِ زَيْدٍ. (قَوْلُهُ: كَالْحَيَوَانِ النَّاطِقِ) أَيْ: فَإِنَّ فِيهِ نِسْبَةً تَقْيِيدِيَّةً، وَهِيَ نِسْبَةُ النُّطْقِ لِلْحَيَوَانِ؛ لِأَنَّ الثَّانِيَ وَهُوَ النَّاطِقُ مُقَيِّدٌ لِلْأَوَّلِ وَهُوَ الْحَيَوَانُ. ثُمَّ إِنَّ ظَاهِرَ الشَّارِحِ أَنَّ هَذَا الْمِثَالَ وَمَا بَعْدَهُ فِيهِ مَوْضُوعٌ وَمَحْمُولٌ، وَنِسْبَةٌ غَيْرُ تَامَّةٍ، وَلَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ، فَلَوْ قَالَ: كَالْحَيَوَانِ النَّاطِقِ حَادِثٌ مَثَلًا، وَغُلَامِ زَيْدٍ فَاضِلٌ، كَانَ أَوْلَى. وَقَدْ يُقَالُ: إِنَّ قَصْدَ الشَّارِحِ التَّمْثِيلُ لِلنِّسْبَةِ التَّقْيِيدِيَّةِ بِقَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ. (قَوْلُهُ: وَغُلَامِ زَيْدٍ) أَيْ: فَإِنَّ فِيهِ نِسْبَةً تَقْيِيدِيَّةً، وَهِيَ نِسْبَةُ الْغُلَامِيَّةِ لِزَيْدٍ؛ لِأَنَّ الثَّانِيَ فِيهَا وَهُوَ زَيْدٌ مُقَيِّدٌ لِلْأَوَّلِ وَهُوَ غُلَامٌ. (قَوْلُهُ: وَإِمَّا تَامَّةٌ) أَيْ: وَهِيَ الَّتِي يَحْسُنُ السُّكُوتُ عَلَيْهَا. (قَوْلُهُ: كَاضْرِبْ) أَيْ: فَفِي اضْرِبْ نِسْبَةُ طَلَبِ الضَّرْبِ إِلَى الْمُخَاطَبِ، وَهِيَ نِسْبَةٌ تَامَّةٌ يَحْسُنُ السُّكُوتُ عَلَيْهَا، وَلَكِنَّهَا غَيْرُ خَبَرِيَّةٍ؛ لِأَنَّ الْخَبَرِيَّةَ تَتَحَقَّقُ بِدُونِ اللَّفْظِ الدَّالِّ عَلَيْهَا، وَهَذِهِ لَا تَتَحَقَّقُ بِدُونِ لَفْظِ اضْرِبْ. (قَوْلُهُ: مَشْكُوكَةٍ) أَيْ: كَمَا إِذَا قُلْتَ: قَامَ زَيْدٌ، وَأَنْتَ شَاكٌّ فِي وُقُوعِ نِسْبَةِ الْقِيَامِ لِزَيْدٍ وَعَدَمِهِ، وَمِثْلُ الْمَشْكُوكَةِ الْمُتَوَهَّمَةُ وَالْمُتَخَيَّلَةُ. قَالَ الْعَطَّارُ: (قَوْلُهُ: إِمَّا تَقْيِيدِيَّةٌ) نِسْبَةٌ لِلتَّقْيِيدِ؛ لِأَنَّ الثَّانِيَ قَيْدٌ فِي الْأَوَّلِ، وَهِيَ صَادِقَةٌ بِالْإِضَافِيَّةِ: غُلَامُ زَيْدٍ، وَبِالتَّوْصِيفِيَّةِ: كَالْحَيَوَانِ النَّاطِقِ؛ فَلِذَا أَدْرَجَهُمَا الشَّارِحُ تَحْتَهَا. (قَوْلُهُ: كَاضْرِبْ) وَكَذَلِكَ بَقِيَّةُ صُوَرِ الْإِنْشَاءِ، وَكَوْنُ صُوَرِ الْإِنْشَاءِ مُتَضَمِّنَةً لِنِسْبَةٍ خَبَرِيَّةٍ غَيْرُ مَنْظُورٍ إِلَيْهِ؛ لِأَنَّ الْمَدَارَ عَلَى الْمَدْلُولِ الْوَضْعِيِّ لَهَا لَا لِلَازِمِهِ.
Either restrictive: like 'the speaking animal' and 'the servant of Zayd'. Or complete: non-informative like 'strike!', or informative but uncertain. al-Dasuqi said: His statement 'either restrictive' is the relation upon which it is not proper to remain silent, and one of the two matters is in it, which is the second as a restriction for the first, and it is two types: descriptive, which is where the second is a description of the first, like the relation in 'the speaking animal', and additive, which is where the second is in the construct state to it, like the relation in 'the servant of Zayd'. His statement 'like the speaking animal' means that there is a restrictive relation in it, which is the relation of speech to the animal, because the second, which is 'speaking', restricts the first, which is 'animal'. Then the apparent meaning of the commentator is that this example and what follows have a subject and predicate, and an incomplete relation, but it is not so. If he had said 'like the speaking animal is an event' and 'the servant of Zayd is virtuous', it would have been better. It may be said that the aim of the commentator is to give an example of the restrictive relation without considering the two extremes. His statement 'and the servant of Zayd' means that there is a restrictive relation in it, which is the relation of servitude to Zayd, because the second, which is Zayd, restricts the first, which is servant. His statement 'and either complete' means that it is the one upon which it is proper to remain silent. His statement 'like strike!' means that in 'strike!' there is the relation of request of hitting to the addressee, and it is a complete relation upon which it is proper to remain silent, but it is non-informative because the informative is realized without the indicative word, while this is not realized without the word 'strike!'. His statement 'uncertain' means as when you say 'Zayd stood' while you are doubting the occurrence of the relation of standing to Zayd or its non-occurrence, and similar to the uncertain are the conjectural and the imaginary. al-'Attar said: His statement 'either restrictive' is a relation of restriction because the second is a restriction on the first, and it applies to the additive: 'the servant of Zayd', and to the descriptive: like 'the speaking animal'; therefore the commentator included both under it. His statement 'like strike!' and similarly the rest of the forms of composition, and the fact that the forms of composition include an informative relation is not considered because the focus is on their conventional meaning, not on its implication.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies types of relative propositions (nisab). It opens with the matn stating that nisab are either restrictive (taqyidiyya) or complete (tamma). The restrictive relation is one where the second term qualifies the first, and it is further divided into descriptive (tawsifiyya) like 'the speaking animal' where 'speaking' describes 'animal', and additive (idafiyya) like 'the servant of Zayd' where 'Zayd' is in the construct state. The commentator al-Dasuqi explains that a restrictive relation cannot stand alone as a complete utterance; one cannot remain silent after uttering just 'the speaking animal'. He then critiques the matn's examples because they appear to have a subject and predicate, which would be a complete relation, but the matn intends them only as examples of the relation itself without considering the extremes. The complete relation is then defined: it is the relation upon which it is proper to remain silent, i.e., it forms a complete utterance. Examples include the imperative 'strike!' (non-informative) and an informative clause where the truth is uncertain, like 'Zayd stood' when one is in doubt. al-'Attar adds that the restrictive relation covers both descriptive and additive, and that forms of composition (insha) like commands are treated based on their conventional meaning, not on any underlying informative relation they may imply.

Key Terms
نسبة
تقييدية
تامة
توصيفية
إضافية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a restrictive additive (idafiyya) relation?
A) The speaking animal (الحيوان الناطق)
B) The servant of Zayd (غلام زيد)
C) Strike! (اضرب)
D) Zayd stood (قام زيد)

Key Takeaway
Relations are either restrictive (qualifying a term) or complete (forming a full utterance); this classification is foundational for analyzing propositions in Islamic logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Classify the following Arabic phrases as restrictive (descriptive or additive) or complete (informative or non-informative): (a) 'the messenger of Allah' (رسول الله), (b) 'Allah is the Creator' (الله الخالق), (c) 'do not drink wine' (لا تشرب الخمر), (d) 'the generous scholar' (العالم الكريم).

Critique of Universal Judgment on 'Alam
Al-Attar criticizes the view that 'ālam is a collective noun and the problem of universal judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical error of conflating a universal judgment (ḥukm kullī) with a collective whole, using al-Attar's critique of 'ālam.

Core Idea
Universal judgments apply to each individual separately, not to the group as one unit.

This passage focuses on a critical logical issue in kalam: when we say 'the world is temporal', does the judgment apply to every individual thing or to the world as a collective? Al-Attar exposes a contradiction in a position that tries to use intuitive knowledge of some individuals to prove a universal claim. Understanding this distinction is vital for evaluating arguments about the creation of the world.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term under dispute
The term 'ālam (world) is being analyzed. Is it a universal genus/species or a collective noun referring to a whole?
2
Distinguish universal from collective
A universal judgment like 'every human is animal' applies to each individual human. A collective judgment like 'the army is strong' applies to the group as a unit. The difference is crucial.
The author notes that in 'the world is temporal', the subject is not each individual but the universal meaning.
3
Analyze the proponent's argument
The proponent claims the temporality of all genera is theoretical, yet some individuals' temporality is intuitive. He then tries to support the universal claim with these intuitive cases.
This mixes levels: intuitive knowledge of some does not prove universal theoretical knowledge.
4
Pinpoint the contradiction
The proponent earlier said the judgment is on the whole (like 'everyone carries the rock'), but later relies on intuitive knowledge of individuals. The author calls this tahāfut (contradiction).
If the judgment is universal, it must be supported for each individual; if it's collective, intuitive cases are irrelevant.
5
Recognize the correct logical demand
For a universal affirmative proposition, every individual must be included. The proponent's move from some to all is invalid without a universal link.
This is a classic logical error: hasty generalization or fallacy of composition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جنس من تلك لفظُ عالم؛ أجناس العوالم وأنواعها وثبوت الحدوثِ لِكُلِّ الأجناس نظري ؛ وإنْ كانَ أفَرادُ تلكَ الأجناس الشَّخصيَّةِ كُلُّ واحدٍ منها حدوثه بديهيا، والقائلونَ بِقِدَمِ العَالَمِ مُعتَرِفُونَ بذلك، والقديم عندَهُم هُوَ الأنواع على تفصيل في ذلك عندهم، ومعلوم أنَّ الفردَ الشَّخصيَّ في قولنا : العَالَمُ حَادِثُ، غيرُ مَنظورٍ إليه حالةَ الحُكْمِ؛ لأنَّهُ غيرُ مَدلولٍ لِلّفظ الموضوع، فإرادة الفردِ الشَّخصيّ لا دليل عليهِ، على أنَّ في كلامِهِ اختلالاً مِن وُجُوعٍ أُخَرَ، فَإِنَّهُ إِذا أُريدَ الإيجاب الكُلَّيُّ وأنَّ المعنى: كُلُّ فردٍ فرد ... إلخ، كما قال ؛ يكونُ الحُكْمُ مُنصبا على تلك الأفرادِ، وقَوْلُهُ بعد ذلكَ : إِذْ لا شَكٍّ أنَّ العِلْمَ بحدوثِ بعض الأفرادِ ضروريّ ؛ يقتضي أنَّ الحُكْمَ الإيجابيَّ حُكم على المجموع؛ كَكُلِّ يحملون الصخرة، وقَدْ فرَّرَ قبلَهُ أَنَّهُ مِن قَبيلِ الحُكْمِ على الجَميعِ؛ تَكُلِّ إنسان حيوان، وهَل هذا إِلَّا تَهَافُت؟!، وقولُ بعض الحواشي : إِنَّ العَالَمَ اسمٌ لِمجموع جميعِ مَا سِوَى الله تعالى .ا.هـ. خلافُ المختارِ كَمَا سَمِعْت . بني تميم
A genus of those... the term 'ālam; the genera of the universes and their species and the proof of temporality for all genera is theoretical, even if each individual of those individual genera has its temporality intuitive. Those who assert the eternity of the world acknowledge that, but the eternal according to them is the species, with detail in their view. It is known that the individual person in our statement 'the world is temporal' is not considered at the time of judgment because it is not the referent of the term used. So intending the individual has no evidence for it. Moreover, there is a confusion in his statement from other aspects: if universal affirmation is intended meaning 'every individual...' as he said, then the judgment applies to those individuals. His statement after that: 'since there is no doubt that knowledge of the temporality of some individuals is intuitive' would require that the affirmative judgment is a judgment on the whole, like 'everyone carries the rock'. And he had previously decided that it is of the type of judgment on all, like 'every human is an animal'. Is this not a contradiction?! And the statement of some glosses: 'Indeed, 'ālam is a name for the totality of all besides Allah'. End quote. Contrary to the chosen view, as you have heard. [Beni Tamim]
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of a position regarding the term 'ālam (the universe) and universal judgments. The author (al-Attar) examines a claim that the temporality of the world is known theoretically for all genera, even though the temporality of each individual is intuitive. He points out that in the proposition 'the world is temporal', the term 'ālam does not refer to individual particulars; rather, it refers to the universal concept. If one intends a universal affirmative (all individuals), then the judgment applies to each individual, but the proof offered for some individuals' temporality being intuitive does not establish the universal claim because one cannot generalize from some to all without a logical link. The author detects a contradiction: the earlier statement that the judgment is on the whole (like 'every human is an animal') conflicts with the later claim that knowledge of some individuals' temporality is intuitive. He calls this 'tahāfut' (contradiction). Finally, he mentions that some define 'ālam as 'all besides Allah', but he rejects that as contrary to the chosen view. This critique highlights the importance of proper logical analysis in theological discussions about the world's creation.

جنس

Key Takeaway
Universal judgments require evidence for each individual member; using intuitive knowledge of some individuals is insufficient and leads to contradiction.

Types of Strange Accident (al-‘Arad al-Gharib)
Al-Dasuqi defines al-‘arad al-gharib with three types and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given accidental property as essential or strange, and if strange, identify the type (more specific, more general, or completely separate intermediary).

Core Idea
A strange accident attaches to a subject via a more specific, more general, or separate intermediary.

Not every property of a thing belongs to its essence. Some properties come from outside—these are called 'strange accidents'. In this lesson, we will explore how to recognize them and classify them based on the relationship between the intermediary and the subject. This skill is crucial for defining the scope of a science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and the accident
Given a statement like 'Laughter attaches to animal via humanity', note the subject (animal) and the accident (laughter). Also note the intermediary (humanity).
The intermediary is the means through which the accident arrives.
2
Determine if the accident is essential or strange
If the accident attaches directly to the essence of the subject, it is essential. If it attaches through an intermediary, it is strange. Here, laughter attaches via humanity, so it is strange.
Essential accidents are studied in the science of the subject.
3
Classify the intermediary relative to the subject
The intermediary can be more specific (akhass), more general (a'amm), or completely separate (mubayin). 'Humanity' is more specific than 'animal', so laughter is a strange accident of animal via something more specific.
Use the examples: laughter (via more specific), movement (via more general), color/heat (via separate).
4
Apply to other examples
Test with an example from fiqh: 'Intoxication attaches to khamr via its intoxicating property.' Intoxication is an essential accident of khamr? No, it attaches via a more specific property (being an intoxicant that causes intoxication). So it is strange via more specific.
Always check the intermediary and its relation to the subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأَمَّا فِي الثَّالِثِ؛ فَلانَّ العارض اللاحق بواسطة أمرٍ مساوٍ؛ مستند لذلك الأمر المساوي، والمساوي مُستند لِلذَّاتِ، والمستند إلى المستند إلى شيء؛ مستند لذلك الشَّيء. والعَرَضُ الغريبُ؛ ما يكونُ لحوقُه للمعروضِ بواسطةِ أمرٍ أخصَّ؛ كَالضَّحكِ اللاحقِ للحيوانِ بواسطةِ كونه إنساناً، وهو أخص أو أعم؛ كَالتَّحَرُكِ اللَّاحِقِ لِلإِنسَانِ بواسطةِ كونه حيواناً، أو مُباين له؛ كَاللَّونِ العارضِ للجسمِ بواسطةِ السَّطحِ، وكالحرارةِ اللاحقةِ للماءِ بواسطةِ النَّارِ، وبينَ الماءِ والنَّارِ تباينٌ. وإنَّما سُمِّيت غَريبةً؛ لأنَّها وإن كانت عارِضةً للمعروضِ ليستْ مُستندةً لذاتِه، فهي غَريبةٌ وبعيدةٌ عن ذاتِه. وإنَّما كان يُبحث في الفنِّ عن الأعراضِ الذاتيَّةِ للشيء دون أعراضِه الغريبةِ؛ لأنَّ أعراضَه الذاتيَّةَ أحوالٌ له في الحقيقةِ، فلذا يُبحث في الفنِّ المُتعلِّقِ بِه عنها بخلافِ أعراضِه الغريبةِ، فإنَّها في الحقيقة ليست أحوالاً له، وإنَّما هي أحوالٌ للغيرِ الذي ثَبَتَتْ لذلك الشيءِ بِسَبَبِه، فلا يُبحث عنها في الفنِّ المُتعلِّقِ بذلك الشيءِ، وإنَّما يُبحث عنها في الفنِّ المُتعلِّقِ بذلك الغيرِ؛ لأنَّ المقصودَ في كلِّ علمٍ إنَّما البحثُ عن أحوالِ موضوعِه الحقيقيَّةِ.
As for the third [type, it is] because the accident that attaches via something equal is dependent on that equal thing, and the equal is dependent on the essence, and that which is dependent on something dependent on something is dependent on that thing. And the strange accident is that whose attachment to the subject is via something more specific, like laughter attaching to animal via its being human, and it is more specific or more general, like movement attaching to human via its being animal, or [something] separate from it, like color attaching to body via surface, and like heat attaching to water via fire, and between water and fire there is separation. And it was called 'strange' because even though it attaches to the subject, it is not dependent on its essence, so it is strange and distant from its essence. And indeed, in a discipline, only the essential accidents of a thing are investigated, not its strange accidents, because its essential accidents are truly states of it; thus, in the discipline pertaining to it, they are investigated, unlike its strange accidents, for they are truly not states of it, but rather states of the other thing by which they are established for that thing; so they are not investigated in the discipline pertaining to that thing, but are investigated in the discipline pertaining to that other thing, because the purpose in every science is only the investigation of the real states of its subject.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage explains the concept of 'strange accident' (al-‘arad al-gharib) and its classification. It begins by discussing a third scenario (relevant to a prior discussion) where an accident attaches to a subject via something equal to that subject; the chain of dependency is traced back to the essence. The main definition then states that a strange accident is one that attaches to a subject via something more specific (akhass), more general (a‘amm), or completely separate (mubayin). Examples are given: laughter attaches to animal via the more specific 'human', movement attaches to human via the more general 'animal', and color attaches to body via surface or heat attaches to water via fire—the latter two are separate (mubayin) because surface and fire are not essentially related to the body or water. The term 'strange' is justified because such accidents are not dependent on the essence of the subject; they are remote from it. The passage then provides a methodological principle: each science only investigates the essential accidents of its subject, because those are genuinely states of the subject. Strange accidents are not states of the subject itself but states of something else (the cause) that happen to be attached to the subject; therefore, they are studied in the science of that cause, not in the science of the subject itself. This principle is foundational for demarcating scientific subject matters.

Key Terms
العَرَضُ الغريبُ
العَرَضُ الذَّاتِيُّ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Strange accident via something more general
D) Strange accident via something separate

Key Takeaway
To classify an accident, identify the intermediary: if it is more specific, more general, or separate, the accident is strange and belongs to the science of the intermediary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following: 'Visibility attaches to the moon via its being illuminated by the sun.' Is visibility an essential or strange accident of the moon? If strange, what type?
?

5 min
Why Each Science Studies Only Essential Accidents
Al-Dasuqi explains why strange accidents are not studied in the science of their subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the principle that a science investigates only the essential accidents of its subject and apply it to determine the proper scientific domain for a given accident.

Core Idea
A science studies only the real states of its subject; strange accidents are states of another thing.

In this lesson, we examine the reasoning behind the rule that each science excludes 'strange' accidents. The passage argues that strange accidents are not genuine states of the subject—they are states of the intermediary cause. Therefore, they belong to the science of that intermediary. This principle is fundamental for defining the boundaries of disciplines like logic, physics, fiqh, and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Trace the chain of dependency
Start with the accident, e.g., heat on water. Heat depends on fire, which is separate from water. So heat is dependent on fire, not on water's essence.
If the accident depends on something else, it is not a state of the subject itself.
2
Identify the 'real' subject of the accident
The accident's true subject is the intermediary it depends on. For heat, the real subject is fire that caused it; for color, it is the surface that receives it.
The accident 'attaches' to the original subject only via the intermediary.
3
Determine which science studies it
Each science studies the essential states of its own subject. The science of water does not study heat; the science of fire (or the subject that includes fire) studies heat.
This prevents overlap: the science of the original subject leaves out properties that belong to other sciences.
4
Apply to a kalam example
Consider the accident 'being created' for the world. It is an essential accident of the world (since the world depends on a creator). Therefore, it is studied in kalam, the science of the world's essential states. But a particular event like 'burning', although it occurs in the world, depends on fire as an intermediary—so it is studied in physics, not kalam.
This helps delineate the subject matter of kalam: only essential accidents of 'the world in itself'.

وأَمَّا فِي الثَّالِثِ؛ فَلانَّ العارض اللاحق بواسطة أمرٍ مساوٍ؛ مستند لذلك الأمر المساوي، والمساوي مُستند لِلذَّاتِ، والمستند إلى المستند إلى شيء؛ مستند لذلك الشَّيء. والعَرَضُ الغريبُ؛ ما يكونُ لحوقُه للمعروضِ بواسطةِ أمرٍ أخصَّ؛ كَالضَّحكِ اللاحقِ للحيوانِ بواسطةِ كونه إنساناً، وهو أخص أو أعم؛ كَالتَّحَرُكِ اللَّاحِقِ لِلإِنسَانِ بواسطةِ كونه حيواناً، أو مُباين له؛ كَاللَّونِ العارضِ للجسمِ بواسطةِ السَّطحِ، وكالحرارةِ اللاحقةِ للماءِ بواسطةِ النَّارِ، وبينَ الماءِ والنَّارِ تباينٌ. وإنَّما سُمِّيت غَريبةً؛ لأنَّها وإن كانت عارِضةً للمعروضِ ليستْ مُستندةً لذاتِه، فهي غَريبةٌ وبعيدةٌ عن ذاتِه. وإنَّما كان يُبحث في الفنِّ عن الأعراضِ الذاتيَّةِ للشيء دون أعراضِه الغريبةِ؛ لأنَّ أعراضَه الذاتيَّةَ أحوالٌ له في الحقيقةِ، فلذا يُبحث في الفنِّ المُتعلِّقِ بِه عنها بخلافِ أعراضِه الغريبةِ، فإنَّها في الحقيقة ليست أحوالاً له، وإنَّما هي أحوالٌ للغيرِ الذي ثَبَتَتْ لذلك الشيءِ بِسَبَبِه، فلا يُبحث عنها في الفنِّ المُتعلِّقِ بذلك الشيءِ، وإنَّما يُبحث عنها في الفنِّ المُتعلِّقِ بذلك الغيرِ؛ لأنَّ المقصودَ في كلِّ علمٍ إنَّما البحثُ عن أحوالِ موضوعِه الحقيقيَّةِ.
As for the third [type, it is] because the accident that attaches via something equal is dependent on that equal thing, and the equal is dependent on the essence, and that which is dependent on something dependent on something is dependent on that thing. And the strange accident is that whose attachment to the subject is via something more specific, like laughter attaching to animal via its being human, and it is more specific or more general, like movement attaching to human via its being animal, or [something] separate from it, like color attaching to body via surface, and like heat attaching to water via fire, and between water and fire there is separation. And it was called 'strange' because even though it attaches to the subject, it is not dependent on its essence, so it is strange and distant from its essence. And indeed, in a discipline, only the essential accidents of a thing are investigated, not its strange accidents, because its essential accidents are truly states of it; thus, in the discipline pertaining to it, they are investigated, unlike its strange accidents, for they are truly not states of it, but rather states of the other thing by which they are established for that thing; so they are not investigated in the discipline pertaining to that thing, but are investigated in the discipline pertaining to that other thing, because the purpose in every science is only the investigation of the real states of its subject.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage explains the concept of 'strange accident' (al-‘arad al-gharib) and its classification. It begins by discussing a third scenario (relevant to a prior discussion) where an accident attaches to a subject via something equal to that subject; the chain of dependency is traced back to the essence. The main definition then states that a strange accident is one that attaches to a subject via something more specific (akhass), more general (a‘amm), or completely separate (mubayin). Examples are given: laughter attaches to animal via the more specific 'human', movement attaches to human via the more general 'animal', and color attaches to body via surface or heat attaches to water via fire—the latter two are separate (mubayin) because surface and fire are not essentially related to the body or water. The term 'strange' is justified because such accidents are not dependent on the essence of the subject; they are remote from it. The passage then provides a methodological principle: each science only investigates the essential accidents of its subject, because those are genuinely states of the subject. Strange accidents are not states of the subject itself but states of something else (the cause) that happen to be attached to the subject; therefore, they are studied in the science of that cause, not in the science of the subject itself. This principle is foundational for demarcating scientific subject matters.

Key Terms
العَرَضُ الغريبُ
العَرَضُ الذَّاتِيُّ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does a science not investigate the strange accidents of its subject?
A) Because they are not real properties
B) Because they are states of something else, not the subject itself
C) Because they are too difficult to study
D) Because they are always unknown

Key Takeaway
The scope of a science is defined by the essential accidents of its subject; strange accidents are delegated to the science of their intermediary cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the property 'being moved by a human' attached to a stone via the human's push. Is this an essential or strange accident of the stone? Which science would study it?
?
In fiqh, the property 'being sold by exchange' attaches to a commodity via the contract of sale. Is that essential or strange to the commodity? Which science (fiqh or economics) would investigate it?

The Entailment of Mutabaqah by Tadammun and Iltizam
Author explains entailment of mutabaqah by tadammun and iltizam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why tadammun and iltizam entail mutabaqah, even in estimation, and identify the role of intention in this entailment.

Core Idea
Mutabaqah is always implied by the other two significations, even if only hypothetically.

In this passage, the commentator builds on the definitions of the three types of signification to explain their logical relationships. The key point is that while mutabaqah stands alone, both tadammun and iltizam necessarily imply the existence of a mutabaqah relation, even if the word is not directly used for the whole meaning. This entailment holds whether or not the speaker's intention is considered a condition for signification, thanks to the concept of 'estimation' (taqdir).

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of signification
Recall the definitions: mutabaqah is word indicating the entire meaning; tadammun is indicating a part; iltizam is indicating an external concomitant.
These definitions are given earlier in the original text.
2
Locate the entailment claim
The matn states: 'وَتَلْزَمُهُمَا الْمُطَابَقَةُ، وَلَوْ تَقْدِيراً' (And mutabaqah is entailed by the two, even if in estimation). This is the core statement.
3
Understand the two levels of entailment
Commentary clarifies that the entailment is actual (tahqiqan) because whenever tadammun or iltizam occurs, mutabaqah is present at least potentially. The phrase 'even if in estimation' covers cases where the word is used only for a part or concomitant by positing a hypothetical intention for the whole meaning.
4
Examine the underlying dispute about intention
Al-'Attar explains that 'estimation' accommodates the view that signification requires speaker's intention. Without intention, mutabaqah would not be actual, but it can be assumed. The stronger view dispenses with intention; the entailment holds on both views.
This shows how the commentary handles a point of disagreement within the tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذ قد فَرَغَ من تحديدِ الدَّلالاتِ الثَّلاثِ؛ شرع في بيانِ التَّلازم بينهما وعَدَمِه، فقال: (وَتَلْزَمُهُمَا) أي: التَّضمن والالتزام؛ (المطَابَقَةُ، وَلَوْ تَقْدِيراً). الدسوقي: (قَوْلُهُ: وَإِذْ قَدْ فَرَغَ) أي: حين قد فرغ عن تحديد؛ أي: تعريف الدلالات. (قَوْلُهُ: وَتَلْزَمُهُمَا الْمُطَابَقَةُ) أي تحقيقاً؛ إذ متى تحقَّقَتِ التَّضَمُّنِيَّةُ أو الالتزاميَّةُ؛ تحقَّقَتِ المطابقيَّةُ، فيكونانِ مُستلزمين لها، وهذا ما قبل المبالغة. (قَوْلُهُ: وَلَوْ تَقْدِيراً) أي: تلزمُهُما ولو تقديراً؛ أي: حيثُ لم يستعمل اللَّفْظَ في معناه المطابقيّ بالفعل، وإنَّما استعمله في جزئه أو لازمه، فإنَّهُ دال عليه بالمطابقة بتقدير إرادته منه. العطار: وهذا مبنى على أنَّ الدَّلالة الوضعية تتوقَّفُ على الإرادة، وهذا مرجوح عند أهل الفنّ، والمعتبرُ عندَهُم أنَّ اللفظ يدلُّ على معناه الموضوع له؛ سواءٌ حصلت إرادة لهُ أَمْ لَا، فقوله: (وَلَو تقديراً)؛ القصد بهذا الإشارة لبيان استلزامِ التَّضمُّنِيَّةِ والالتزامية للمطابقة على مذهب من يشترط الإرادة فى الدَّلالة الوضعية.
And when he finished from defining the three significations, he began to explain the entailment between them and its absence, so he said: (And they two entail) i.e., tadammun and iltizam; (al-mutabaqah, even if in estimation). Al-Dasuqi: (His saying: 'And when he finished') i.e., when he finished from defining, i.e., defining the significations. (His saying: 'And they two entail al-mutabaqah') i.e., actually; for whenever the tadammuniyyah or iltizamiyyah is realized, the mutabaqiyyah is realized, so they are two that are requiring of it, and this is before the exaggeration. (His saying: 'even if in estimation') i.e., they entail it even if in estimation; that is, when the word is not used in its mutabaqi meaning actually, but rather is used in its part or its necessary concomitant, then it is indicative of it by mutabaqah by estimation of intending it from it. Al-'Attar: And this is based on the fact that wad'iyyah signification is dependent on intention, and this is weak according to the people of the art, and what is relied upon with them is that the word signifies its meaning for which it was placed, whether intention of it occurs or not. So his saying: (even if in estimation) the purpose with this is to indicate the requiring of tadammuniyyah and iltizamiyyah for mutabaqah for the position of one who stipulates intention in wad'iyyah signification.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on the classic matn statement about the relationship between the three types of signification (dalalah). The text first sets the context: after defining the three types—mutabaqah (correspondence between word and whole meaning), tadammun (implication of a part), and iltizam (necessary concomitant)—the author now explains the entailment (tazamum/luzum) between them. The core claim is that tadammun and iltizam entail mutabaqah, but not vice versa. The commentary by al-Dasuqi clarifies that the entailment is actual (tahqiqan) because whenever a word signifies by inclusion or commitment, the correspondence signification is necessarily present, at least potentially. The phrase 'even if in estimation' (wa law taqdiran) addresses cases where the word is used for a part or a necessary consequence rather than the full meaning; in such cases, the word still has a correspondence signification 'by estimation'—meaning that if the full meaning were intended, the word would signify by mutabaqah. Al-'Attar then discusses the underlying dispute: some scholars (like the Shaykh) hold that wad'iyyah (conventional) signification requires the speaker's intention (iradah); thus, without intending the whole meaning, the word cannot be said to signify by mutabaqah. The phrase 'even if in estimation' accommodates that view by speaking of a hypothetical intention. However, the preferred view (mu'tabir) among logicians is that intention is not a condition; the word signifies its meaning by virtue of its original placement regardless of intention. Thus the passage synthesizes both perspectives, ensuring the entailment holds on either account.

Key Terms
الدلالة المطابقيّة
الدلالة التضمنية
الدلالة الالتزامية
تقدير
الإرادة
التلازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following correctly describes the entailment relationship?
A) Mutabaqah entails both tadammun and iltizam.
B) Tadammun and iltizam entail mutabaqah, and mutabaqah also entails them.
C) Tadammun and iltizam entail mutabaqah, even if only by estimation.
D) Tadammun and iltizam do not entail mutabaqah; they are independent.

Key Takeaway
Tadammun and iltizam always imply mutabaqah, either actually or hypothetically through taqdir, regardless of the position on intention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Provide a concrete example from fiqh where a word is used in its tadammuni sense (indicating a part of its meaning) but still entails mutabaqah. Explain the entailment using the concept of taqdir.
?

Seven Categories of Singular Expressions
Commentary explains the seven divisions of singular expressions: proper noun, univocal, ambiguous, etc.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the seven categories of singular expressions and determine whether they apply to nouns, verbs, or particles.

Core Idea
Seven categories classify singular expressions by meaning and scope.

In this lesson, we explore the second division of singular expressions introduced by the commentator. These seven categories—proper noun, univocal, ambiguous, equivocal, transferred, literal, and metaphorical—are essential for understanding how terms function in Islamic logic. The commentary also clarifies that some categories apply beyond nouns to verbs and particles, while others are restricted to nouns due to the nature of those expressions.

Guided Path
1
List the seven categories
From the passage, extract the seven types: proper noun (علم), univocal (متواطئ), ambiguous (مشكك), equivocal (مشترك), transferred (منقول), literal (حقيقة), metaphorical (مجاز).
2
Understand each category's definition
Using the key terms, define each: proper noun designates a specific individual; univocal applies equally; ambiguous applies with priority; equivocal has multiple unrelated meanings; transferred shifts from literal; literal is original meaning; metaphorical is figurative with relation.
Definitions are from the commentary's explanations.
3
Determine scope of each category
The commentary states that equivocation, transference, literal, and metaphorical occur in verbs and particles too, but proper noun, univocal, and ambiguous do not because verbs and particles lack universality and particularity.
Mir Zāhid's reasoning clarifies the limitation.
4
Apply the tool to a concrete example
Take the term 'ṣalāh' in Islamic law: originally 'prayer', transferred to ritual prayer—this is a transferred term (منقول). Recognizing this prevents treating it as literal in all contexts.
Real example from Usul al-Fiqh.

(والمفرد ينقسم أيضاً) إلى أقسام العَلَمُ، والمتواطئ والمشكّكُ ، والمشترك، والمنقولُ ، والحقيقة، والمجاز ؛ لأنَّه :
(قَوْلُهُ : أَيْضَاً) أي: كَمَا انقسم المفردُ إلى ما سبق، وقوله: (ينقسم)؛ أي: : باعتبار معناه، وقوله: (إلى أقسام)؛ أي: سبعة وجَعْلُ هذا التقسيم للمفردِ لَا للاسم وَلَا لِلمستقل ؛ مأخوذ من قول المصنِّفِ : (أيضاً)؛ لأنَّ فيه تنبيهاً على أنَّ هذا تقسيم ثانٍ، والذي قُسّمَ أوَّلاً : المفرد؛ لا الاسم وَلَا المستقل واعلَمْ أنَّ الاشتراكَ والنَّقل والحقيقة والمجاز؛ كما تجري في الاسم؛ تجري في الفعل والحرف.
(قَوْلُهُ : وَالمُفْرَدُ يَنْقَسِمُ ) أخذَ قَولَهُ: (والمفرد) ؛ مِن قَوْلِ المصنفِ أيضاً؛ لأنَّ فِيهِ التنبية على أنَّ هذا تقسيم ثانوي، فليس تقسيماً للاسم ولا للمستقل؛ إذْ لَمْ يُسبَق لهما تقسيم، ثمَّ إِنَّ المقسَّمَ مُطلَقُ كما قاله الجلالُ ؛ لا المفرد المطلق، وعَلَّلَهُ مير زاهد بأنَّ كُلَّا مِنَ الكلمةِ والأداة لا يكونُ عَلَماً ولا متواطئاً ولا مُشككاً، فإنَّهما لا يَتَّصِفَانِ بالكلية والجزئية، وإذا جعل المفرد المطلق مُقسَّماً ؛ يلزمُ أنْ يكونَ كُلٌّ مِنَ الكلمةِ
Section: On investigations of expressions. (And the singular also divides) into divisions: the proper noun, the univocal, the ambiguous, the equivocal, the transferred, the literal, and the metaphorical. (His statement: 'also') i.e., just as the singular divided into what preceded, and his statement: 'divides' i.e., according to its meaning, and his statement: 'into divisions' i.e., seven. And making this division for the singular, not for the noun nor for the independent, is taken from the author's statement 'also' because it contains an indication that this is a second division, and what was divided first is the singular, not the noun nor the independent. And know that equivocation, transference, literal, and metaphorical, just as they occur in nouns, also occur in verbs and particles. (His statement: 'And the singular divides') He took his statement 'and the singular' from the author's statement also, because it contains an indication that this is a secondary division, so it is not a division for the noun nor for the independent, since no division preceded for them. Then, the divided is absolute as al-Jalal said, not the absolute singular. And Mir Zāhid explained it: that each of the word and the tool cannot be a proper noun, univocal, or ambiguous, because they are not characterized by universality and particularity. And if the absolute singular is made the divided, it would be necessary that each of the word...
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage introduces a section on the investigation of expressions. It presents the second division of singular expressions into seven categories: proper noun (ʿalam), univocal (mutawāṭiʾ), ambiguous (mushakkik), equivocal (mushtarak), transferred (manqūl), literal (ḥaqīqah), and metaphorical (majāz). The commentary clarifies that this division applies to the singular as a general category, not specifically to the noun or to the independent word, because the first division was already for the singular. The commentator then notes that some of these categories (equivocation, transference, literal, metaphorical) also apply to verbs and particles, not just nouns. However, categories like proper noun, univocal, and ambiguous do not apply to verbs and particles because these parts of speech do not possess universality or particularity (i.e., they are not terms that can be predicated of many). The commentary ends with an explanation from Mir Zāhid that if we took the absolute singular as the divided, it would require that every word (including tools and verbs) be classifiable into these categories, which is false. The text cuts off before completing the argument.

The Non-Disparity of Word Divisions
Al-'Attar denies disparity between word divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how a single Arabic word can belong to multiple divisions (proper noun, univocal, amphibolous) depending on the meaning considered.

Core Idea
A word's classification depends on which meaning you look at.

In this passage, al-'Attar explains that the divisions of lafz (word) are not mutually exclusive. A single word can be a proper noun from one perspective, univocal from another, and amphibolous from a third. This is crucial for understanding how words function in Islamic sciences like usul al-fiqh, where terminology must be precisely defined.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three divisions mentioned
Find the Arabic terms for proper noun (علم), univocal (متواطئ), and amphibolous (مشكك). Understand each one's meaning from the passage.
2
Analyze the perspective
The passage says 'with respect to one meaning' (نظراً إلى معنى). Consider a word like 'وجود' (existence). How is it amphibolous? How can it also be univocal in a different context?
3
Examine reality and metaphor
The passage adds that reality (حقيقة) and metaphor (مجاز) also follow the same principle. Provide an example where a word is used both literally and metaphorically.

(قَوْلُهُ: لَا عَارِضَاً) الأنسب أن يقول: لَا عُرُوضاً.
العطار
لا تَبَايُنَ بينَ هذهِ الأقسام إذْ يوصَفُ اللَّفْظُ الواحد بالعلمية نظراً إلى معنى، وبالتّواطُئِ نَظراً إلى آخر، وبالتَّشكيكِ نظراً إلى آخر، والحقيقة والمجاز كَذلِكَ؛ انتهى. والجواب: أنَّا نَختارُ الشَّقَّ الأَوَّلَ، وأنَّ المراد المعنى الحقيقي كما نَبَّه عليه عبد الحكيم، وعَلَّلَهُ بأَنَّهُ لو كانَ مَجازاً لَكَانَ مَعناه كثيراً لامتناع تحقُّقِ المعنى المجازي بدونِ المعنى الحقيقي، وأنَّ معنى قَوْلِهِ: فَمَعَ تَشخُصِهِ وضعاً اعتبارُ التَّشخُص فيما وضع له، فيكون جزئيا حقيقيًّا كَمَا صرَّحَ بِهِ مير زاهد، وأنَّ في ضمير كثر معناه استخدامًا بأنْ يُرادَ بِهِ مُطلَقُ المعنى؛ فثبت بذلك صحَّةُ الاحتياج لقوله: وضعاً، لإفادته جزئيَّةَ المعنى؛ فإنَّ المعنى الحقيقي الموضوعَ له قد يكونُ كُلِّيَّا، وتناول التَّقسيم المجازَ بالنَّظَرِ لإرادةِ عموم المعنى في قوله: وإِنْ كَثُرَ مَعناه، واستغنيتُ بذلك عمَّا ذكرَهُ مِنَ الجوابِ باختيارِ الشَّقِّ الثَّاني، فإنَّهُ مَعَ كَوْنِهِ مُخالِفاً لِمَا حَقَّقُوهُ مِن أَنَّ المراد المعنى المطابقي، ويشعرُ بهِ أيضاً قولُ الشَّارح: إن اتَّحد معناه، حيثُ عبر بالاتحادِ مع إضافة المعنى لِلّفظ؛ فإنَّ المراد معنَّى له مزيد اختصاص بِهِ كما تفيده الإضافة، ولا يكون ذلكَ إلَّا لِلمعنى الحقيقي؛ لم يُشفِ غليلاً، وَلَا حاجةَ لكَ بعد هذا إلى مَا طَوَّلَ بِهِ بعضُ الحواشي مِنَ التَّأويلاتِ والتَّرديداتِ.
(قَوْلُهُ: لَا عَارِضَاً) أي: بواسطة الاستعمالِ كَمَا في المضمراتِ وأسماء الإشارة ونظائرهما؛ بناءً على مختارِ المصنّف فيها مِنْ أنَّها كُلِّيَّاتٌ وَضْعاً؛ جُزئيات استعمالاً، وقد سبق أنَّ التَّقسيمَ هُنا جارٍ على ذلك الاصطلاح، فيكون التقييد بذلك لإخراجِهَا عَنِ العَلَم، فإِنَّ التَّشخُصَ في مدلولاتها ليسَ مِنَ الوضع، بَل مِنَ الاستعمالِ، وَهَهُنا بحث أورده مير زاهد على المذهب الذي حققه المتأخرون فيها، وهو أَنَّهُ مُخَالِفٌ لِمَا ذهب إليهِ الشَّيْخُ وكثيرٌ مِنَ المحققينَ مِنْ أَنَّ الألفاظ موضوعةٌ
(۱) (قولُ العطَّارِ: فيكونُ ... إلخ) فيه أنَّ الموضوع لهُ كُلّي عند المصنِّفِ، وهو يُنافي التّشخُصَ قطعاً، فهي خارجةٌ بِهِ لا بـ: وضعاً، كما لا يَخفَى ا.هـ. الشَّرنوبي.
الخارجي
Chapter: On Investigations of Words
'Not accidental';
Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'not accidental') It is more appropriate to say 'not occurrence'.
Al-'Attar: There is no disparity between these divisions, for one word may be described as a proper noun with respect to one meaning, as univocal with respect to another, and as amphibolous with respect to another. And reality and metaphor are likewise. End. And the answer: We choose the first alternative, and that what is meant is the real meaning, as 'Abd al-Hakim pointed out and justified by saying that if it were metaphorical, its meaning would be many because the metaphorical meaning cannot be realized without the real meaning. And that the meaning of his statement: 'then with its individuation wad'an' is the consideration of individuation in what was placed for it, so it is real particular as Mir Zahid stated. And that in the pronoun, 'its meaning is many' is an usage in the sense that the absolute meaning is intended. Thus the need for his statement 'wad'an' is established, because it indicates the particularity of the meaning; for the real meaning assigned may be universal. And the division includes metaphor by way of intending the generality of meaning in his statement: 'and if its meaning is many'. And I dispense with what he mentioned of the answer by choosing the second alternative, for while it contradicts what they verified that what is meant is the meaning in accordance with the wording, and this is also suggested by the commentator's statement: 'if its meaning is one', where he expressed with 'union' while adding the meaning to the word; for what is intended is a meaning that has more specificity to it as the addition indicates, and that can only be for the real meaning; it did not quench the thirst. And you have no need after this for the lengthy interpretations and hesitations of some glosses.
(His statement: 'not accidental') i.e., by way of usage, as in pronouns, demonstratives, and their likes, based on the author's choice that they are universals in wad', particulars in usage. And it has preceded that the division here proceeds on that convention. So the restriction is to exclude them from being proper nouns, for the individuation in their denotations is not from wad' but from usage. And here there is a discussion presented by Mir Zahid on the school that the later scholars verified about them, that it contradicts what the Shaykh and many of the verifiers held that the words are placed with a general wad' for a specific subject.
(Note 1) (Al-'Attar's statement: 'so it becomes... etc.') In it, the subject for which it is placed is universal according to the author, and this absolutely contradicts tashakhkhus, so they are excluded by it, not by 'wad'an', as is not hidden. End. Al-Sharnubi. Al-Khariji.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is from a new chapter on investigations of words (فصل في مباحث الألفاظ). It begins by asserting that there is no disparity (تباين) between the various divisions of words, such as proper noun (علم), univocal (متواطئ), amphiboly (مشكّك), reality (حقيقة), and metaphor (مجاز). A single word can be described with different divisions depending on which meaning is considered. For example, the word 'وجود' (wujud) is amphibolous when applied to God and creation (priority and posteriority), but it can also be considered univocal in a certain sense. Similarly, a word can be both a proper noun (like 'مكة') and also be used metaphorically. The commentator then presents an answer to an objection: they choose the first alternative, that what is meant in the definition is the real meaning (المعنى الحقيقي). This is justified by the fact that metaphorical meaning depends on real meaning, and if it were metaphorical, the meaning would be multiple. The phrase 'then with its individuation wad'an' is interpreted as considering individuation in what was placed for it, resulting in a real particular, as Mir Zahid stated. The pronoun's 'many meanings' is an instance of usage where the absolute meaning is intended. Thus the restriction 'wad'an' is necessary to indicate the particularity of the meaning, because the real meaning assigned can be universal. The division includes metaphor by intending the generality of meaning in the phrase 'and if its meaning is many'. The commentator dismisses the alternative answer as inadequate. Then the discussion returns to the phrase 'not accidental' (لا عارضاً). It is explained that the individuation is not accidental in the sense that it comes from usage, as in pronouns etc. That is why the restriction was made to exclude them from being proper nouns. Finally, a note by al-Sharnubi points out a contradiction: if the subject for which the word is placed is universal (as the author holds), then individuation is impossible, so these words are excluded by the condition of tashakhkhus, not by 'wad'an'. This is an ongoing debate.

Key Terms
علمية
تواطؤ
تشكيك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-'Attar, why is there no disparity between the divisions of lafz?
A) Because all words are ultimately univocal.
B) Because a single word can be described with different divisions depending on the meaning considered.
C) Because the divisions are not real categories.
D) Because proper nouns are always univocal.

Key Takeaway
The divisions of lafz are not mutually exclusive; a single word can belong to multiple categories when considered from different perspectives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the Arabic word 'قرآن' (Qur'an), explain how it can be considered a proper noun (as the specific revelation) and also univocal (as any recitation).
?
Classify the word 'مكة' (Mecca) according to the divisions: is it a proper noun, univocal, or amphibolous? Justify.

intermediate
7 min
The Necessity of 'Wad'an' in Definitions
Commentator justifies the inclusion of 'wad'an'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the restriction 'wad'an' (by convention) is necessary to indicate the particularity of meaning in a definition.

Core Idea
Wad'an ensures the meaning is particular, not universal.

In the source, the commentator defends the inclusion of 'wad'an' in the definition of a proper noun. Without it, the definition might accidentally include universal meanings. This lesson unpacks the debate over whether the intended meaning is real (haqiqi) or metaphorical, and how 'wad'an' resolves it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key phrase
Find the phrase 'مع تشخصه وضعاً' (with its individuation wad'an). Understand that 'wad'an' means by convention, i.e., from the original linguistic assignment.
2
Understand the objection
Some argue that if the meaning were metaphorical, then the word would have many meanings. Why does the commentator reject that?
3
See the justification
The commentator says that 'wad'an' indicates that the individuation is from the wad', not from usage. This ensures that the meaning is a real particular, not a universal.

Pronouns and Demonstratives: Universal in Wad', Particular in Usage
Debate on pronouns as proper nouns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between wad' (linguistic convention) and usage (استعمال) in determining the particularity of words like pronouns and demonstratives.

Core Idea
Some words are universal in wad' but particular in usage.

The passage discusses that pronouns (e.g., 'هو') and demonstratives ('هذا') are considered universals in their original linguistic convention (wad'an), but they become particulars in usage. This nuance affects whether they qualify as proper nouns ('alam). Understanding this helps clarify the boundaries of the definition of 'alam.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of 'alam
A proper noun is a word that refers to a specific individual by convention. The passage adds the condition that the individuation must be from wad', not usage.
2
Understand the debate
Mir Zahid points out that pronouns and demonstratives are universal in wad' (they are general categories), but they become particular when used (e.g., 'هذا' points to a specific object).
3
Apply the condition
Because their particularity comes from usage, they are excluded from being proper nouns by the condition 'wad'an'. The note by al-Sharnubi argues that they are excluded by 'tashakhkhus' instead.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مباحثِ الألفاظ
لا عارضاً؛
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ: لَا عَارِضَاً) الأنسب أن يقول: لَا عُرُوضاً.
العطار
لا تَبَايُنَ بينَ هذهِ الأقسام إذْ يوصَفُ اللَّفْظُ الواحد بالعلمية نظراً إلى معنى، وبالتّواطُئِ نَظراً إلى آخر، وبالتَّشكيكِ نظراً إلى آخر، والحقيقة والمجاز كَذلِكَ؛ انتهى. والجواب: أنَّا نَختارُ الشَّقَّ الأَوَّلَ، وأنَّ المراد المعنى الحقيقي كما نَبَّه عليه عبد الحكيم، وعَلَّلَهُ بأَنَّهُ لو كانَ مَجازاً لَكَانَ مَعناه كثيراً لامتناع تحقُّقِ المعنى المجازي بدونِ المعنى الحقيقي، وأنَّ معنى قَوْلِهِ: فَمَعَ تَشخُصِهِ وضعاً اعتبارُ التَّشخُص فيما وضع له، فيكون جزئيا حقيقيًّا كَمَا صرَّحَ بِهِ مير زاهد، وأنَّ في ضمير كثر معناه استخدامًا بأنْ يُرادَ بِهِ مُطلَقُ المعنى؛ فثبت بذلك صحَّةُ الاحتياج لقوله: وضعاً، لإفادته جزئيَّةَ المعنى؛ فإنَّ المعنى الحقيقي الموضوعَ له قد يكونُ كُلِّيَّا، وتناول التَّقسيم المجازَ بالنَّظَرِ لإرادةِ عموم المعنى في قوله: وإِنْ كَثُرَ مَعناه، واستغنيتُ بذلك عمَّا ذكرَهُ مِنَ الجوابِ باختيارِ الشَّقِّ الثَّاني، فإنَّهُ مَعَ كَوْنِهِ مُخالِفاً لِمَا حَقَّقُوهُ مِن أَنَّ المراد المعنى المطابقي، ويشعرُ بهِ أيضاً قولُ الشَّارح: إن اتَّحد معناه، حيثُ عبر بالاتحادِ مع إضافة المعنى لِلّفظ؛ فإنَّ المراد معنَّى له مزيد اختصاص بِهِ كما تفيده الإضافة، ولا يكون ذلكَ إلَّا لِلمعنى الحقيقي؛ لم يُشفِ غليلاً، وَلَا حاجةَ لكَ بعد هذا إلى مَا طَوَّلَ بِهِ بعضُ الحواشي مِنَ التَّأويلاتِ والتَّرديداتِ.
(قَوْلُهُ: لَا عَارِضَاً) أي: بواسطة الاستعمالِ كَمَا في المضمراتِ وأسماء الإشارة ونظائرهما؛ بناءً على مختارِ المصنّف فيها مِنْ أنَّها كُلِّيَّاتٌ وَضْعاً؛ جُزئيات استعمالاً، وقد سبق أنَّ التَّقسيمَ هُنا جارٍ على ذلك الاصطلاح، فيكون التقييد بذلك لإخراجِهَا عَنِ العَلَم، فإِنَّ التَّشخُصَ في مدلولاتها ليسَ مِنَ الوضع، بَل مِنَ الاستعمالِ، وَهَهُنا بحث أورده مير زاهد على المذهب الذي حققه المتأخرون فيها، وهو أَنَّهُ مُخَالِفٌ لِمَا ذهب إليهِ الشَّيْخُ وكثيرٌ مِنَ المحققينَ مِنْ أَنَّ الألفاظ موضوعةٌ
(۱) (قولُ العطَّارِ: فيكونُ ... إلخ) فيه أنَّ الموضوع لهُ كُلّي عند المصنِّفِ، وهو يُنافي التّشخُصَ قطعاً، فهي خارجةٌ بِهِ لا بـ: وضعاً، كما لا يَخفَى ا.هـ. الشَّرنوبي.
الخارجي
Chapter: On Investigations of Words
'Not accidental';
Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'not accidental') It is more appropriate to say 'not occurrence'.
Al-'Attar: There is no disparity between these divisions, for one word may be described as a proper noun with respect to one meaning, as univocal with respect to another, and as amphibolous with respect to another. And reality and metaphor are likewise. End. And the answer: We choose the first alternative, and that what is meant is the real meaning, as 'Abd al-Hakim pointed out and justified by saying that if it were metaphorical, its meaning would be many because the metaphorical meaning cannot be realized without the real meaning. And that the meaning of his statement: 'then with its individuation wad'an' is the consideration of individuation in what was placed for it, so it is real particular as Mir Zahid stated. And that in the pronoun, 'its meaning is many' is an usage in the sense that the absolute meaning is intended. Thus the need for his statement 'wad'an' is established, because it indicates the particularity of the meaning; for the real meaning assigned may be universal. And the division includes metaphor by way of intending the generality of meaning in his statement: 'and if its meaning is many'. And I dispense with what he mentioned of the answer by choosing the second alternative, for while it contradicts what they verified that what is meant is the meaning in accordance with the wording, and this is also suggested by the commentator's statement: 'if its meaning is one', where he expressed with 'union' while adding the meaning to the word; for what is intended is a meaning that has more specificity to it as the addition indicates, and that can only be for the real meaning; it did not quench the thirst. And you have no need after this for the lengthy interpretations and hesitations of some glosses.
(His statement: 'not accidental') i.e., by way of usage, as in pronouns, demonstratives, and their likes, based on the author's choice that they are universals in wad', particulars in usage. And it has preceded that the division here proceeds on that convention. So the restriction is to exclude them from being proper nouns, for the individuation in their denotations is not from wad' but from usage. And here there is a discussion presented by Mir Zahid on the school that the later scholars verified about them, that it contradicts what the Shaykh and many of the verifiers held that the words are placed with a general wad' for a specific subject.
(Note 1) (Al-'Attar's statement: 'so it becomes... etc.') In it, the subject for which it is placed is universal according to the author, and this absolutely contradicts tashakhkhus, so they are excluded by it, not by 'wad'an', as is not hidden. End. Al-Sharnubi. Al-Khariji.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is from a new chapter on investigations of words (فصل في مباحث الألفاظ). It begins by asserting that there is no disparity (تباين) between the various divisions of words, such as proper noun (علم), univocal (متواطئ), amphiboly (مشكّك), reality (حقيقة), and metaphor (مجاز). A single word can be described with different divisions depending on which meaning is considered. For example, the word 'وجود' (wujud) is amphibolous when applied to God and creation (priority and posteriority), but it can also be considered univocal in a certain sense. Similarly, a word can be both a proper noun (like 'مكة') and also be used metaphorically. The commentator then presents an answer to an objection: they choose the first alternative, that what is meant in the definition is the real meaning (المعنى الحقيقي). This is justified by the fact that metaphorical meaning depends on real meaning, and if it were metaphorical, the meaning would be multiple. The phrase 'then with its individuation wad'an' is interpreted as considering individuation in what was placed for it, resulting in a real particular, as Mir Zahid stated. The pronoun's 'many meanings' is an instance of usage where the absolute meaning is intended. Thus the restriction 'wad'an' is necessary to indicate the particularity of the meaning, because the real meaning assigned can be universal. The division includes metaphor by intending the generality of meaning in the phrase 'and if its meaning is many'. The commentator dismisses the alternative answer as inadequate. Then the discussion returns to the phrase 'not accidental' (لا عارضاً). It is explained that the individuation is not accidental in the sense that it comes from usage, as in pronouns etc. That is why the restriction was made to exclude them from being proper nouns. Finally, a note by al-Sharnubi points out a contradiction: if the subject for which the word is placed is universal (as the author holds), then individuation is impossible, so these words are excluded by the condition of tashakhkhus, not by 'wad'an'. This is an ongoing debate.

Key Terms
عارض
وضع
استعمال
تشخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why are pronouns excluded from being proper nouns according to the author?
A) Because they are not words.
B) Because their particularity comes from usage, not from wad'.
C) Because they are always universal.
D) Because they have no meaning.

Key Takeaway
Pronouns and demonstratives are universal in wad' but particular in usage, so they do not qualify as proper nouns because proper nouns require particularity from wad'.

Gradation of Existence and the Debate on Substance Intensity
Commentary defines 'أولوية' as gradation and debates whether substance admits intensity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the Illuminationist and Peripatetic positions on whether substance admits intensity and identify the inconsistency in the Peripatetic view.

Core Idea
Intensity is perfection of essence for Illuminationists; Peripatetics deny it for substance but inconsistently allow it for magnitude.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks the term 'أولوية' (priority/gradation) and dives into a major metaphysical dispute: does substance admit intensity? You will learn how two schools define 'شدة' and how their assumptions lead to different conclusions, with a surprising inconsistency in the Peripatetic position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions of 'أولوية'
The passage distinguishes ordinary priority from special 'أولوية' meaning completeness/perfection. Find that distinction in the first sentence.
Al-Dasooqi glosses 'أولوية' as 'special in the sense of completeness and perfection'.
2
Understand the Illuminationist view on intensity
Note that they treat increase, power, and intensity as the same reality—perfection of essence—named differently by category (quantity, substance, quality).
This is from the quote of Sharh Sullam al-Ulum: 'الزيادة والقوة والشدة أمر واحد وهو كمال الماهية'.
3
Analyze the Peripatetic objection and contradiction
The Peripatetics distinguish these terms, but the commentator points out their inconsistency: they allow 'longer' (increase in length) but not 'more intense as a line', even though length is the line.
This is a key logical critique demonstrating internal inconsistency.
4
Evaluate the arguments on substance intensity
Illuminationists affirm based on observable perfection (elephant vs. mosquito) and direct experience through spiritual exercises. Peripatetics deny based on common usage, but the commentator notes they allow increase/decrease for magnitude, which they consider a substance.
Consider which side offers stronger evidence: experiential vs. linguistic custom.

والقُوَّةُ والسِّدَّةُ أمرٌ واحد وهو كمالُ الماهية، لكن إذا وُجِدَتْ في الكمّ سُمِّيَتْ زيادةً، وإذا وُجِدَتْ في الجوهرِ سُمِّيَتْ قُوَّةً، وإذا وُجِدَتْ في الكَيْفِ سُمِّيَتْ شِدَّةٌ، وَكَذا أضدادُهَا ، لكنَّ هذهِ إطلاقات عرفيَّةٌ لا اعتدادَ بِهَا في العلوم الحُكميَّةِ، والمشّاؤونَ قالُوا بتغايرها ؛ نظراً إلى الإطلاقات، وناقضُوا أنفسَهُم؛ حيثُ لم يُجوِّزُوا كونَ الخطّ  َأشدَّ خَطَّيَّةً، وجوَّزُوا أَشدَّ طُولاً ، مَعَ أَنَّ الطُّول هو الخَطُّ ، ثم قال : واختلفُوا هَل الجوهرُ يشتد أم لا؟ قالَ الإشراقيُّونَ : نَعَمْ، وهو ظاهر، فإنَّهم عَنَوا بها كمال الماهيَّة، والماهيَّةُ الجوهريَّةُ So it is in the Necessary Being before its occurrence in the contingent. Al-Dasooqi: 'or priority' (with jarr), coordinated with his statement 'priority'; i.e., the disparity is either... (His statement: 'or priority') i.e., special in the sense of completeness and perfection, unlike the priority [mentioned earlier]. Al-Attar: They interpreted 'intensity' as the multiplicity of the universal's effects in some individuals, and it is not hidden that it entails gradation in the essentials like 'human'. Al-Jalal in his gloss on al-Tajrid indicated its answer: that the meaning of one of the two individuals being more intense is that it is such that the mind, with the help of imagination, abstracts from it instances of the weaker, and analyzes it into them by a kind of analysis. So the concept of 'black' is said with gradation of two specific blacks, considering that blackness in one is more than in the other, in the sense that the mind with the help of imagination abstracts from one instances of the other. And in Sharh Sullam al-Ulum: The Illuminationists said: increase, power, and intensity are one thing, which is the perfection of the essence, but when found in quantity it is called increase, when found in substance it is called power, when found in quality it is called intensity, and likewise their opposites. But these are conventional usages not considered in the philosophical sciences. The Peripatetics said they are different, looking at the usages, and they contradicted themselves: they did not allow a line to be 'more intense as a line' but allowed 'longer', even though length is the line. Then he said: They disagreed whether substance admits intensity. The Illuminationists said yes, and it is apparent, for they meant by it perfection of essence, and the substantial essence in an elephant is more perfect than in a mosquito, due to the appearance of the effects of multiplicity in the elephant without it. And according to what the followers of the Peripatetics interpreted, there is also allowance, for it is possible that some separate entities are such that instances of other separate entities are abstracted from them, and no proof indicates the contrary. The Illuminationists claimed direct experience through spiritual exercises. The Peripatetics said substance does not admit intensity, and they did not provide proof; rather they based it on customary usage, since it is not said of a substance that it is more intense than another substance. And increase and decrease, according to their own principle, substance also admits them, for they said magnitude is a substance, and it admits them. (End quote). And it is not body with (His statement: 'So it is in the Necessary Being before its occurrence in the contingent') i.e., the Necessary existence is prior to contingent existence with essential priority, because the second is an effect arising from the first.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the concept of 'أولوية' (priority/gradation) as applied to existence. The author begins by glossing the term 'أَوْ أَوْلَوِيَّةٍ' (or priority) as meaning 'special in the sense of completeness and perfection', contrasting it with ordinary priority. Al-Attar then notes that some explain intensity (شدة) as multiplicity of the universal's effects in individuals, but this would entail gradation in essential attributes like 'human'. He cites al-Jalal's answer: the more intense individual is such that the mind can abstract from it instances of the weaker, so blackness can be said with gradation. This leads to a lengthy quote from Sharh Sullam al-Ulum comparing Illuminationist and Peripatetic views. The Illuminationists hold that increase, power, and intensity are one reality—perfection of essence—differently named in quantity, substance, and quality. The Peripatetics distinguish them and are accused of contradiction, e.g., allowing 'longer' (increase in length) but not 'more intense as a line', even though length and line are the same. The debate then focuses on whether substance (جوهر) can have intensity. Illuminationists affirm it because manifest perfection differs (elephant vs. mosquito). Peripatetics deny it based on common usage, but the commentator notes they inconsistently allow increase and decrease for magnitude, which they consider a substance. The passage concludes by reaffirming the essential priority of Necessary existence over contingent existence, as the latter is an effect of the former.

أولوية
شدة
جوهر

Key Takeaway
The dispute on intensity in substance reveals a deeper methodological divide: Illuminationists rely on metaphysical perfection as a real attribute, while Peripatetics restrict intensity to qualities based on linguistic custom, but their own principles allow it in magnitude, exposing an inconsistency.

Why Geometric Quantities Are Accidents
Explanation of why geometric quantities are accidents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why point, line, and surface are classified as accidents in Aristotelian-Islamic logic and test the definition using the criterion of independent existence.

Core Idea
Limits do not subsist independently; they are accidents inhering in substances.

In Islamic logic, understanding the ontological status of mathematical objects is essential for categorizing knowledge. This passage asserts that geometric quantities—point, line, and surface—are accidents because they exist only as limits (nihayat) and extremities (atraf) of magnitudes. Let's examine the reasoning behind this classification and see how it fits into the broader framework of substance and accident.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The author states that 'all of them are accidents' (والكل أعراض). Determine who 'all of them' refers to—the point, line, and surface.
The passage focuses on these three, though the teaching body is also discussed.
2
Extract the reason
The reason given is 'because they are ends and extremities of magnitudes' (لأنها نهايات وأطراف للمقادير). Recognize that 'ends' implies they do not have independent existence; they are boundaries of something else.
Magnitudes here are continuous quantities like lines, surfaces, and bodies.
3
Understand the criterion for accident
An accident ('arad) is defined as something that subsists in a substance (jawhar) and cannot exist independently. The passage clarifies that these quantities are non-independent in existence.
This aligns with the Aristotelian substance-accident ontology adopted by Islamic philosophers.
4
Apply the criterion to the examples
Consider the point: it is the end of a line and has no extension. The line is the end of a surface, and the surface is the end of a body. None exist independently; they require a magnitude to limit.
The teaching body itself is not a limit; it is the magnitude that has limits.
5
Note the controversy
The passage mentions that philosophers (hukama') affirm the reality of continuous magnitude, while theologians (mutakallimun) deny it. This affects the status of these quantities.
In Islamic theology, the existence of continuous quantity is debated; some consider it a mental abstraction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكلُّ أعراض غير مستقلة الوجود؛ لأنَّها نهايات وأطراف للمقادير على ما بُيِّنَ في كتب الحِكمَةِ .
الدسوقي: (قَوْلُهُ : غَيْرُ مُسْتَقِلَّةِ الْوُجُوْدِ) أي: لا تقومُ بِنَفْسِهَا ، أو إنَّما تقوم بالجوهر . وقولُهُ: (لأنّها نهايات)؛ أي: لأنَّ مجموعها نهايات، وإلا؛ فالجسم التعليمي ليس نهايةَ الشَّيء، تأمل. (قَوْلُهُ: وَأَطْرَافٌ لِلْمَقَادِيْرِ) أي: الخط والشطح، والجسم التعليمية، وهي الامتدادات القائمة بالجواهر، وهي الخطُ الطَّبيعي، والشطحُ الطَّبيعي، والجسم
العطار: (قَوْلُهُ : وَالْكُلُّ) أي : النُّقطة والخطُّ والسّطح، لكنَّ الخطّ والشطحَ بِاتِّفاق؛ لأنهما مِنَ المقدارِ المعرَّف بأنَّهُ الكَمُ المُتَّصِلُ القارُّ الذَّات المنقسم إلى الجسم التعليمي والسطح، وأمَّا النُّقطةُ؛ فسيأتي الكلام فيها، والمثبت للمقدار هو الحكماء، والمتكلمونَ نَفوهُ، ثمَّ إِنَّ الجسمَ التَّعليمي ينتهي بالسطح، وهو بالخطّ بالنُّقطة سُمِّيَ جسماً تعليميا؛ لأنَّهُ مَوضوعُ العُلومِ التَّعليميَّةِ؛ كالسطح والخطّ التَّعليميَّيْنِ، وهذا الجسم التعليمي هو الكَمِّيَّةُ القائمة بالجسمِ الطَّبيعي، وهو الجسم المتحيّز الشارية فيه، ويُسمى باعتبارِ كَونِهِ حَشواً مَا بينَ السَّطوح : ثخناً، وباعتبار كونه نازلاً من فوق : عُمقاً، وباعتبارِ كَونِهِ صَاعداً من تحت سمكاً، وقولُ المحشي : والكُلُّ : أعراض؛ أي: للجسمِ التَّعليميّ، وكذلِكَ قوله بعدَ أنْ نقل عبارةَ شرحِ الطَّوالعِ : وَبِهِ تعلمُ مَا في كلامِ الشَّارح بالنسبة للجسمِ التّعليميّ؛ حيثُ جعله نهاية لِلمقدارِ؛ عدول عن الصَّوابِ، أَمَّا الأَوَّلُ؛ فَلَمَّا بَيَّنَّا أَنَّها قائمة بالجسم الطَّبيعي، وأمَّا الثَّاني؛ فَليسَ كلامِ الشَّارح ذِكْرُ الجسمِ التّعليميّ، بَلْ إِنَّما ذكرَ الخطَّ والسَّطح . (قَوْلُهُ : لِأَنَّهَا نِهَايَاتٌ) قال البعضُ : الصَّميرُ - وهو اسم ـ إِنْ وقعَ على النُّقطة والخط والشطح والمقدارِ الَّذي هُوَ كَم؛ أقسامُهُ تلكَ الثَّلاثةُ مَعَ الجسمِ التَّعليمي .ا.هـ. وهذا خطأ فاحش، فإنَّهُ لا يقولُ عاقل فَضْلاً عَنْ فاضل؛ بأنَّ النُّقطةَ مِنْ مَقولة
And all of them are accidents not independent in existence, because they are ends and extremities of magnitudes as explained in the books of wisdom. al-Dasooqi: (His saying: 'non-independent in existence') i.e. they do not subsist by themselves, or rather they subsist by the substance. And his saying: ('because they are ends') i.e. because their totality is ends; otherwise the teaching body is not an end of something, reflect. (His saying: 'and extremities of the magnitudes') i.e. the teaching line, surface, and body, which are extensions subsisting in substances, namely the natural line, natural surface, and natural body. al-Atar: (His saying: 'and all of them') i.e. the point, line, and surface, but the line and surface by agreement because they are among magnitude defined as continuous, fixed, self-dividing quantity divisible into teaching body and surface. As for the point, its discussion will come. The affirmers of magnitude are the philosophers (hukama'); the theologians (mutakallimun) denied it. Then indeed the teaching body ends with the surface, and it ends with the line, and the line ends with the point. It is called teaching body because it is the subject of the mathematical sciences, like the teaching surface and teaching line. This teaching body is the quantity subsisting in the natural body, which is the body possessing extension (mutahayyiz) and occupying space. It is called, considering it as filling the space between surfaces, 'thickness' (thikhn), and considering it descending from above, 'depth' ('umq), and considering it rising from below, 'thickness' (simak). And the words of the commentator (al-muhashshi): 'and all of them are accidents' i.e. of the teaching body. And likewise his statement after quoting from Sharh al-Tawali': 'and by it you know what is in the commentator's speech regarding the teaching body; where he made it an end of magnitude [is] a deviation from correctness. As for the first, because we have shown that they subsist in the natural body, and as for the second, the commentator's speech does not mention the teaching body, but rather he mentioned the line and surface. (His saying: 'because they are ends') Some said: the pronoun - which is a noun - if it falls on the point, line, surface, and magnitude which is quantity; its divisions are those three along with the teaching body. This is a gross error, for no intelligent person, let alone a virtuous one, would say that the point is from the category
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the ontological status of geometric quantities. The main point is that the point, line, surface, and teaching body are accidents (a'rad) because they do not have independent existence; rather, they are limits (nihayat) and extremities (atraf) of magnitudes that inhere in natural bodies (jism tabi'i). Al-Dasooqi clarifies that 'non-independent in existence' means they subsist in substance (jawhar). He also notes that the teaching body is not a limit itself; rather, it is the thing being limited. Al-Atar explains that line and surface are agreed to be magnitudes (miqdar), which is a continuous, fixed, self-dividing quantity. The point is controversial; philosophers affirm magnitude, while theologians deny it. The teaching body is composed of points: it ends with a surface, which ends with a line, which ends with a point. The passage also corrects a mistake: some thought that the pronoun in 'because they are ends' refers to point, line, surface, and magnitude, but this is absurd because the point is not from the category of magnitude. This highlights the precision required in logical definitions. In Islamic thought, these distinctions are crucial for the classification of sciences and the methodology of proof.

Key Terms
أعراض
الجسم الطبيعي
المقدار
الحكماء

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are the geometric quantities (point, line, surface) considered accidents?
A) They are simple and indivisible
B) They are ends and extremities of magnitudes
C) They are substances with independent existence
D) They are the subjects of mathematical sciences

Key Takeaway
Geometric quantities are accidents because they are limits of magnitudes and lack independent existence; this classification is foundational for distinguishing substance from accident in Islamic logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following argument: 'Every teaching body is an accident. The natural body is a substance. Therefore, the teaching body cannot be identical to the natural body.' Using the definition of accident from this lesson, verify whether the premise 'Every teaching body is an accident' is supported by the passage, and then construct a parallel argument using a different geometric quantity (e.g., line or surface) to show its ontological status.
