Teaching Body vs Natural Body: Ontological Distinction
Distinction between teaching body and natural body

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the teaching body (jirm ta'limi) from the natural body (jism tabi'i) and explain how the teaching body is an accident inhering in the natural body.

Core Idea
The teaching body is an accident (quantity) that inheres in the natural body (substance).

In Islamic logic, the term 'body' can refer to two very different things: the natural body, which is a substance occupying space, and the teaching body, which is the abstract continuous quantity studied in mathematics. This passage clarifies that the teaching body is an accident that subsists in the natural body, not a substance itself. Let's break down the distinction and see why it matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of body
The passage distinguishes between 'al-jism al-tabi'i' (natural body) and 'al-jism al-ta'limi' (teaching body). The natural body is a substance with extension; the teaching body is the quantity that inheres in it.
The natural body is the physical object; the teaching body is its mathematical dimension.
2
Define the teaching body
It is described as 'the quantity subsisting in the natural body' and 'the subject of the mathematical sciences'. It is a continuous magnitude that can be divided into surface, line, and point.
The teaching body is not a limit; it is the magnitude that has limits.
3
Understand its accidental nature
The teaching body is an accident ('arad) because it does not exist independently; it exists only as an attribute of the natural body, which is a substance.
This aligns with the Aristotelian substance-accident ontology.
4
Note the different names for the teaching body
Depending on its orientation, it is called 'thickness' (thikhn), 'depth' ('umq), or 'thickness' (simak). These are merely different aspects of the same quantity.
The many names reflect its relation to the natural body.
5
Recognize the correction given by al-Atar
Al-Atar corrects a mistake where someone interpreted the teaching body as an end of magnitude. He explains that the teaching body is the magnitude itself, not its limit.
This illustrates the precision needed in logical definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(قَوْلُهُ: وَأَطْرَافٌ لِلْمَقَادِيْرِ) أي: الخط والشطح، والجسم التعليمية، وهي الامتدادات القائمة بالجواهر، وهي الخطُ الطَّبيعي، والشطحُ الطَّبيعي، والجسم

Three Doctrines on the Point and Its Classification under Genus
Author presents three views on whether the point falls under a genus and explains the correct view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three doctrines regarding the point's relation to genus and explain the logical reasoning for the correct view, including the principle of aspect and the distinction between simple and composite definitions.

Core Idea
A single aspect cannot ground multiple concepts; hence simple entities require multiplicity to be under genus and differentia.

This passage tackles a foundational issue in Islamic logic: can a simple entity like the geometrical point be classified under a genus? The author presents three scholarly positions, endorses the third, and supports it with reasoning and citations from Ibn Sina and al-Farabi. You will learn why simple entities cannot have a proper definition and how the intellect approximates a definition for them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three doctrines
The author compares the point to the unit. List the three positions: (1) they never combine under a genus, (2) they may sometimes combine, (3) they are inseparable. Note which one the author endorses.
2
Understand the author's reasoning
Why is the third doctrine correct? The referent of predication of genus and differentia is the subject itself. A single aspect (haythiyya) cannot be the source of abstraction for multiple concepts. Therefore the subject must have multiplicity, allowing genus and differentia to apply.
This is the key metaphysical principle.
3
Distinguish simple vs. composite definitions
Ibn Sina says definitions have parts, but the defined may be simple and lack parts. For simples, the intellect invents a quasi-genus and quasi-differentia. For composites, genus corresponds to matter, differentia to form. Al-Farabi adds that simple entities have no differentia at all; differentia is only for composites.
This explains why defining the point is only an approximation.
4
Reflect on the role of approximation (musamaha)
The author states that applying genus and differentia to simple entities is 'from the type of approximation' (min qabil al-musamaha). This means that such definitions are not real (haqiqi) but are intellectual constructs.
Recognize that the logical categories of genus and differentia are not fully applicable to simple entities in the strict sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنَّ النُّقطةَ كَالوحدة؛ فيها ثلاثةُ مَذاهب : الأول : لا يجتمعان أصلاً، والثاني : أنَّهما قد يجتمعان والثَّالثُ : أَنَّهما مُتَلازِمَانِ، ومَا يقتضيهِ النَّظرُ الصَّائِبُ والفِكْرُ الثَّاقِبُ ; هُوَ القولُ الثَّالِثُ ; لأنَّ مِصداق حَمْل الجنسِ والفَصْلِ ومنشأ انتزاعهما ليس إلا نفس الموضوع ، ونحن نعلم بالضرورةِ أنَّ الحيثية الواحدة لا تكونُ منشأ الانتزاع للمفهومات المتعدّدة ومصداقاً لِحَمْلِهَا، فَيلزمُ أَنْ يكونَ في نفس الموضوع تكثر ، وَمَا وقعَ مِنْ تَحديدِ البسائط وإطلاق الجنس والفصل لَهَا ; فَمِنْ قَبيلِ المسامحة، قالَ الشَّيخُ في التَّعليقات : الحدُّ لَهُ أجزاء، والمحدود قد لا يكونُ لَهُ أجزاء، وذَلِكَ إذا كان بسيطاً، وحينئذٍ يخترعُ العقلُ شَيئاً يقوم مقام الجنس، وشيئاً يقومُ مَقامَ الفصل، وأمَّا في المركَّب; فإِنَّ الجنسَ يُناسِبُ المادَّةَ، والفصل يُنَاسِبُ الصُّورةَ ، وَقَالَ الفارابي في تعليقاتِهِ : البسائِطُ لا فصلَ لَهَا ; فَلَا فصل لِلونٍ وَلَا لِغيرِهِ مِنَ البسائط، وإنَّما الفصلُ للمركَّبات، وإِنَّما يُحاذى بالفصل الصُّورةُ; كَمَا يُحاذَى بالجنس المادَّة .ا.هـ. وإن أردتَ استيفاء الكلام في هذا المقام ; فَارجِعْ لِحَواشينا التي كتبناها على المقولات. (1) (قَوْلُهُ : عرض (عام) الصواب عرضاً عاماً، بالنصب على أنَّه ونعته مفعول ثانٍ لجعل المبني للمجهول. ا.هـ. الشرنوبي.
Know that the point, like the unit, has three doctrines: First, they never combine. Second, they may sometimes combine. Third, they are inseparable. What sound inquiry and penetrating thought require is the third statement, because the referent of the predication of genus and differentia and the source of their abstraction is nothing but the subject itself. And we know necessarily that one aspect cannot be the source of abstraction for multiple concepts and a referent for their predication. Therefore, there must be multiplicity in the subject itself. What occurs of defining simple entities and applying genus and differentia to them is of the type of approximation. Al-Shaykh said in al-Ta'liqat: 'The definition has parts, but the defined may not have parts, that is when it is simple. Then the intellect invents something that takes the place of genus and something that takes the place of differentia. As for the composite, genus corresponds to matter, and differentia corresponds to form.' Al-Farabi said in his Ta'liqat: 'Simple entities have no differentia; so there is no differentia for color or for other simple entities. Differentia is only for composites. Differentia corresponds to form, just as genus corresponds to matter.' End quote. And if you want to exhaust the discussion on this topic, refer to our glosses written on al-Maqulat. (1) (His statement: 'arad ('am) the correct form is 'aradan 'amman, in the accusative, as it and its attribute are the second object of ju'ila in the passive construction. End quote by al-Sharnubi.)
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage is a definitional discussion of the point (nuqta) and its relationship to genus. The author begins by stating that the point, like the unit (wahda), has three scholarly positions. The first position holds that they (presumably the point and the unit) never fall under a genus together. The second says they may sometimes fall under a genus. The third declares that they are inseparable from genus—meaning they always fall under a genus. The author endorses the third view as the correct one. The reasoning is that the referent (miṣdāq) of predication of genus and differentia and the source (mansha') of their abstraction is the subject itself. A single aspect (haythiyya) cannot be the source of abstraction for multiple distinct concepts, so there must be multiplicity in the subject. Consequently, defining simple entities (basā'iṭ) by genus and differentia is only a loose approximation. The author then quotes Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh) from al-Ta'liqat: a definition has parts, but the defined may be simple and lack parts; in that case, the intellect invents something to serve as genus and something to serve as differentia. For composites, genus corresponds to matter and differentia to form. Al-Farabi adds that simple entities have no differentia at all—color and other simples have no differentia; only composites have differentia. Differentia corresponds to form, genus to matter. The passage concludes by directing the reader to the author's own glosses on the Categories (al-Maqulat) for more detail. The footnote from al-Sharnubi corrects a textual point about the word 'arad (accident) being incorrectly written as 'arad ('am) and should be 'aradan 'amman.

Key Terms
النقطة
الوحدة
جنس
فصل
بسائط
مصداق
حيثية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the third doctrine (that the point and unit are inseparable from genus) correct?
A) Because the point and unit are exactly the same thing.
B) Because a single aspect cannot be the source of abstraction for multiple concepts, so the subject must be multiple, requiring genus and differentia.
C) Because al-Farabi explicitly stated that all entities have a differentia.
D) Because the point and unit both fall under the category of quantity.

Key Takeaway
Simple entities like the point cannot be genuinely defined by genus and differentia; any such definition is an intellectual approximation.

Three Doctrines on Point and Line: A Critical Analysis
Al-Dasuqi outlines three positions on whether point (nuqta) and line (khatt) are accidents or mental entities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three metaphysical doctrines concerning point and line and identify the internal inconsistency in al-Dawwani's position as critiqued by al-Dasuqi.

Core Idea
Point and line: are they real accidents or mental constructs? The answer changes their logical genus.

In this passage, al-Dasuqi presents three positions on the ontological status of point (nuqta) and line (khatt): they are mental considerations, qualities, or accidents without a specific category. He then uses these distinctions to expose a contradiction in al-Dawwani's reasoning. This lesson walks you through the three doctrines and the critique, sharpening your ability to detect inconsistencies in metaphysical definitions that affect logical classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three doctrines
List each doctrine: (1) mental considerations (umur i'tibariyya), so not accidents; (2) category of quality (kayf), thus accidents; (3) accidents but not from the category of quality.
2
Understand the first doctrine's implications
If point and line are mental, they are not real existents, so they cannot fall under the genus of substance or accident. This affects any logical example that treats them as species of accident.
3
Locate al-Dasuqi's critique
He points out that al-Dawwani said 'accident is not a genus for its subordinates,' yet the commentator called point an accident and used it as an example. This is inconsistent if the first doctrine is assumed.
4
Evaluate the consistency
If the first doctrine holds, point and line are not real accidents, so they cannot be used as examples of accidental species. Al-Dawwani's claim might be true, but then the commentator's usage contradicts it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأوَّل: أنَّها مِنَ الأمور الاعتبارية، ومبنى التَّمثيل عليه؛ لأنَّهما على هذا لا يدخلان تحتَ جنس الجوهر والعَرَضِ؛ لأنَّهما قسم من الموجود، والأمورُ الاعتبارية غير موجودة، وبهذا تعلم ما في كلامِ الدَّوَّاني حيثُ قالَ: إِنَّ العرض ليس جنساً لما تحته، وكلامُ الشَّارح ظاهر في موافقتِهِ، فَإِنَّهُ قالَ: والكل أعراض مُستقلّة، فجعلَ النُّقطة عرضاً، ثمَّ ذكر هنا أنَّ التَّمثيل بها مبني على عدمِ اندراجها تحتَ جنس، فاقتضى أنَّ العرض ليس جنساً لهما. المذهبُ الثَّاني: أنَّهما مِن مقولة الكيف، فيكونان داخلَيْن تحتَ جنسِ العرض. المذهبُ الثَّالث: أنَّهما داخلانِ تحتَ جنس العرض، وليسا من مقولة الكيف.
The first: that they are from mental considerations (umur i’tibariyya), and the basis of the example (tamthil) is upon this; because on this view they do not fall under the genus of substance or accident, because they are a division of existence, and mental considerations are not existent. And by this you know the problem in al-Dawwani’s statement where he said: 'Accident is not a genus for what is under it', and the commentator’s statement is apparently in agreement with him, for he said: 'All are independent accidents', and he made the point an accident, then he mentioned here that the example with them is based on their not falling under a genus, so it required that accident is not a genus for them. The second doctrine: that they are from the category of quality (kayf), so they fall under the genus of accident. The third doctrine: that they fall under the genus of accident, but not from the category of quality.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage from al-Dasuqi’s hashiah critically examines three positions regarding the point (nuqta) and line (khatt). The first position holds that they are mental considerations (umur i'tibariyya) and thus do not fall under the genus of substance or accident, as they are merely mental. Al-Dasuqi uses this to critique al-Dawwani and the commentator for inconsistency: al-Dawwani claimed accident is not a genus for its subordinates, while the commentator both called point an accident and built the example on its not falling under a genus. The second position places them under the category of quality (kayf), making them accidental. The third also places them under accident but not specifically under quality. This discussion shows that the logical example using point and line as 'species under accident' is not universally accepted; it depends on one's metaphysical commitments.

مقولة الكيف

Key Takeaway
The ontological status of entities used in logical examples is not neutral; it must align with the definitions of the categories involved, otherwise contradictions arise.

Definition and Division of Separable Accident
Author defines separable accident and partitions it into persisting and ceasing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a separable accident and classify it as persisting or ceasing based on its definition.

Core Idea
A separable accident can leave its subject, unlike a necessary accident.

In this passage, the author defines 'separable accident' (al-ʿaraḍ al-mufāriq) as an accident that can possibly separate from its subject. He then divides it into two types: one that persists for the subject and one that departs. This definition is foundational for distinguishing categories of accidents in Islamic metaphysics and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is 'al-ʿaraḍ al-mufāriq' (the separable accident). Locate it in the Arabic passage.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'accident' (ʿaraḍ), implied as a category. The differentia is 'possible separation' (jā'iz al-infikāk) – it can be separated from its subject.
The definition is: an accident that can possibly separate from its subject.
3
Identify the two divisions
The separable accident is either 'persisting' (yadūmu) for the subject or 'departing' (yazūlu). Note the commentary: persisting can be from the beginning or from the end only.
4
Test the definition and division
Does this definition include all separable accidents? Yes, any accident that can separate falls under it. Does the division cover all possibilities? The commentator argues it must be an exhaustive real disjunction.
This step previews the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في مبادئ التصورات
[العَرَضُ المفارِقُ]:
(وَإِلَّا) عطفٌ على قوله : إن امتنع انفكاكه»؛ أي: وإن لم انفكاكه عن الشَّيء بأن كانَ جائز الانفكاكِ عنه ; فَعَرَضٌ مُفَارِقٌ) والعرض المفارِقُ : إما أن (يَدُوْمَ) للمعروض أو يَزُولَ.
قال الدسوقي: أَي: ابتداءً وانتهاء أو انتهاء لا ابتداء; أي: بأنْ يعلمَ أَنَّهُ يدوم، وبقي ما إذا لم يثبت أصلاً .
وقال العطار: يأتِ بِمَا يُعتَدُّ بِهِ لِفَواتِ الانضباط حينئذ ا.هـ. يعني أَنَّهُ قَدْ قِيْلَ: إِنَّ المنفصلة الواقعة في التقسيم مانعةُ الجمع التي يمكن عدمُ تَحقُّقِ طَرفَيْهَا، فيمكنُ أنْ يكونَ هناكَ قِسْمٌ ثالث، لا أنَّها مُنفصلةً حقيقية لا يمكن عدمُ تَحقُّقِ طَرفَيْهَا ; بَلْ لَا بُدَّ مِنْ تَحقُّقِ واحدٍ مِنْهُمَا، فَلَا يمكن قسم ثالثُ، وهو كلامٌ بعيدٌ عَنِ التَّحقيقِ; فَإِنَّ انضباط الأقسام مقصود في التقسيم، وعلى تقدير أن تكون المنفصلة مانعة جمع ; يفوتُ ذلك .ا.هـ.
Chapter: On the Principles of Concepts
[The Separable Accident]:
(And otherwise) is a conjunct to his statement 'if its separation is impossible'; that is: and if its separation from the thing is not impossible, namely if it is possible for it to separate from it, then it is a separable accident. And the separable accident: either it (persists) for the subject or departs.
Al-Dasuqi said: i.e., from the beginning and end, or from the end not the beginning; meaning that it is known that it persists, and the case when it is not established at all remains.
And al-Attar said: he brings what is reliable because precision is lost then. End quote. He means that it was said: the disjunctive occurring in the division is of the type that prevents conjunction, whose two sides can both be unrealized, so a third division is possible, not that it is a real disjunction whose two sides cannot both be unrealized, but rather one of them must be realized, so no third division is possible. This is a statement far from verification; because precision of divisions is intended in division, and if it is assumed to be a disjunction that prevents conjunction, that precision is lost. End quote.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter titled 'On the Principles of Concepts' (Mabadi' al-Tasawwurat) and defines the separable accident (al-ʿarad al-mufariq). The definition is given in the context of a conditional: if separation from the thing is not impossible (i.e., it can be separated), then it is a separable accident. The separable accident is then divided into two types: one that persists (yadūmu) for the subject, and one that departs (yazūlu). Al-Dasuqi's commentary clarifies that 'persists' can mean persisting from the beginning and end (i.e., for the entire duration), or persisting from the end without the beginning (i.e., it arises and then continues), and that the case where it is never established is excluded (that would not be a separable accident at all). Al-Attar then brings a discussion about the logical form of this division: whether the disjunction is a real disjunction (man' at al-khuluw wa al-jam') or merely a preventing of conjunction. The commentator argues that a real disjunction is required for a proper division that exhausts all possibilities; otherwise, a third division would be possible, and precision is lost. This shows the careful logical analysis applied even to definitions in the classification of accidents.

Key Terms
العرض المفارق
الدوام

B) It is necessarily permanent and unchanging.
C) It can possibly be separated from its subject.
D) It exists only in the mind, not in reality.

Key Takeaway
A separable accident is defined as an accident that can possibly separate from its subject, and it is divided into persisting and ceasing types.

Logical Precision of Divisions: Real vs. Partial Disjunction
Commentator discusses the logical type of disjunction in the division of separable accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a real disjunction (man'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw) and a partial disjunction (man'at al-jam') and evaluate whether a division is exhaustive.

Core Idea
A valid division must use a real disjunction that exhausts all possibilities to be precise.

The commentator Al-Attar raises a subtle point: the division of separable accident into persisting and ceasing could be interpreted as a disjunction that only prevents conjunction (man'at al-jam'), allowing a third category where neither holds. This would make the division imprecise. Understanding this nuance is crucial for constructing logically sound classifications in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the divisory sentence
The division: 'either it persists for the subject or it departs.' Note the word 'either...or' (إما...أو).
2
Understand the two types of disjunction
Man'at al-jam' (prevention of conjunction): cannot both be true, but both can be false. Man'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw (prevention of conjunction and void): exactly one must be true.
The first allows a third option, the second is exhaustive.
3
Analyze Al-Attar's critique
Al-Attar mentions that some said the division uses man'at al-jam', which would allow a third division (none of the two). But he objects: a proper division must be exhaustive, so the disjunction must be the real type.
He calls the alternative view 'far from verification'.
4
Apply to the division
If the division is not exhaustive, then separable accidents could be neither persisting nor ceasing, which contradicts the definition. Therefore the intended reading must be the real disjunction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Defining and Exemplifying the Division of Separable Accidents
Commentary defines and exemplifies the division of separable accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and categorize separable accidents into permanent or transient based on the examples provided by al-Dasuqi.

Core Idea
A separable accident can be permanent like poverty, or transient like shyness; permanence does not imply necessity.

In this lesson, we explore the division of separable accidents (al-'arad al-mufariq) into two types: those that persist indefinitely (permanent) and those that depart either quickly or slowly. The commentator al-Dasuqi provides concrete examples to clarify this division, and we learn the key distinction that 'permanent' here does not mean 'necessary'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories
The text divides separable accidents into permanent (da'im) and transient (za'il). Permanent accidents persist indefinitely with the subject, while transient ones depart at some point.
Permanent does not mean inseparable; it means the accident stays but could theoretically be removed.
2
Extract examples for permanent accidents
The first example is 'permanent poverty' (al-faqr al-da'im). Al-Dasuqi clarifies this means ongoing dependence on other than God. He notes that dependence on God is not separable, so it is not an example.
Al-Dasuqi also distinguishes between general and specific accidents within this example.
3
Extract examples for quickly transient accidents
Two examples are given: the redness of shyness (humrat al-khajal) and the yellowness of fear (sufrat al-wajal). These are temporary physiological states that disappear quickly.
These are familiar, everyday examples that make the concept intuitive.
4
Extract examples for slowly transient accidents
Youth (al-shabab) and gray hair (al-shayb) are given. They depart slowly, over a long period. The text discusses that gray hair can be reversed, citing stories of al-Khidr and Zulaykha.
The slowness is relative; the accident eventually changes or disappears.
5
Understand the logical nuance of 'permanent'
The term 'permanent' here does not mean 'necessary'. A permanent separable accident could theoretically be detached, but in practice it persists. This is a rational possibility.
This nuance is crucial to avoid the objection discussed in a later lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كَالْفَقْرِ الدَّائِمِ، أَوْ يَزُوْلَ عنه بِسُرْعَةٍ كحُمُرةِ الخجل، وصُفْرَةِ الوَجَلِ، أَوْ بُطْءٍ كالشَّبابِ ، والشَّيبِ . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : كَالْفَقْرِ الدَّائِم) أي : كَالافتقارِ لِغيرِ اللهِ الدَّائم، وأمَّا الافتقار إلى الله؛ فهو عرضُ لازم، ثمَّ إِنَّ الافتقار للغير إن اعتبرته بالنّسبة للإنسان؛ كان عرضاً عاما ؛ لأنَّهُ يُقالُ عليه وعلى غيره من أنواع الحيوان، وإن اعتبرته بالنسبة للحيوان؛ كانَ خاصا ؛ لأنَّهُ لَا يخلو عنه دون غيرِهِ. (قَوْلُهُ : كَحُمْرَةِ الْخَجَل ) أي : كَـ : الحُمْرةِ الحاصلة عند الخجل»؛ أي: الحياءُ (وصفرة الوجل)؛ أي : الصُّفرة الحاصلة عند الوجل؛ أي: الخوف. (قَوْلُهُ : كَالشَّبَابِ وَالشَّيْبِ أي : الهرم، وظاهرُهُ أَنَّ كُلا منهما يزولُ بعد بطء، أمَّا الأوّلُ؛ فَظاهر، وأمَّا الثَّاني؛ فَلِأنَّ الشَّيب يزولُ بالشَّبابِ كما وردّ أَنَّ الخَضِرَ العطار يُمكِنُ برُؤُهَا مِنْ تفرُّقِ الاتصال وغيره، وبالقابل ما يزولُ مع بقاء الموضوع، لم يرد ذلك ا.هـ. قاله أبو الفتح، وأجابَ المحقِّقُ الرَّازِيُّ في شَرْحِ المطالعِ بأنَّ الدَّوامَ قدْ يخلو عَن الضَّرورةِ في الجزئيَّاتِ، وإِنَّما لَا يَخلو عنها في الكُلِّيَّاتِ، فَيجوزُ أنْ يثبت عرضُ مُفارقٌ دائماً لِجزئي مِن جُزئيَّاتِهِ مَعَ انفكاكِهِ عنه، وَرَدَّهُ السَّيِّدُ في حَوَاشِيهِ؛ بأنَّ اللُّزوم المذكورَ هَهُنَا عبارةٌ عَن الضَّرورة بالمعنى الأعمّ، وَلَا شَكٌّ أَنَّ الدَّوامَ لا ينفكُ عَن الضَّرورةِ بِهَذا المعنى مطلقاً؛ سواء كانَ في الجزئي أو الكُلّيّ، والفرقُ المذكورُ على تقديرِ تَمَامِهِ؛ إِنَّما هو في الدَّوامِ بالقياس إلى الضَّرورةِ النَّاشِئَةِ عَنِ الذَّاتَ عَلَى مَا قالُوا ثم أجابَ عَنْ أصل الإشكالِ بأنَّ تقسيم العرض المفارق إلى الدائم والزَّائل تقسيم عقليّ ؛ لِتَجويز العقل أنْ يكونَ مَا لَا يمتنع انفكاكه عَنِ الماهية ثابتاً لها دائماً ؛ لِجَوازِ انفكاكِ الدَّوامِ عَنِ الضَّرورةِ في بادئ الرأي وإِنْ لم يكن جائِزَاً في نَفْسِ الأمر .ا.هـ. وفي حاشية مير زاهد : الظَّاهِرُ أنَّ هذا التقسيمَ مَبني على قولهم : الدَّائمةُ أعمُ مُطلقاً مِنَ الضَّروريَّةِ .ا.هـ. (قَوْلُهُ : كَالشَّبَابِ وَالشَّيْبِ ظاهرُهُ أَنَّ كُلَّا مِنْهُمَا يَزولُ، أَمَّا الْأَوَّلُ: فَوَاضِحُ، وأَمَّا الثَّاني: فَفِيهِ نَظَر، وأُجِيبُ بأنَّ الشَّيبَ قد يزولُ بِالشَّبابِ؛ كَمَا وردَ أَنَّ الخَضِرَ بعد مُضيّ كلّ مئةٍ وعشرين سنة عليه يزولُ هرمه ويعودُ له شبابه، وَكَما وردَ : أَنَّ زليخا رجعت إلى شبابها عند تزوج يوسفَ عليه السَّلامُ بها على القول بأنَّهُ تَزوَّجَهَا . وقال بعضُهم : قوله : كـ: «الشَّباب، والشَّيْب»؛ لعلَّ المرادَ كَـ: «الشَّبابِ معَ الشيب»، فإنَّهُ يزولُ بِهِ، فَالمثالُ واحد . (قَوْلُهُ : فَإِنْ قِيلَ : ( هذا السؤال وارد على قول المصنف، وإلا ، فَمُفارقٌ ، ثم تقسيمه إلى كونه يدوم أو يزول؛ فَبِحسَبِ الظَّاهِرِ لا يصحُ التَّقسيم العطار . عليهِ السَّلامُ بعدَ مُضيّ مئة وعشرين سنةٌ عليه يعود إلى الشَّبابِ، ويكفي هذا الفردُ لِصعَّةِ زوالِ الشَّيبِ بِالشَّبابِ، وكَذَا مَا ورد أنَّ زليخا رجعت إلى شبابها عند تزوج يوسف عليه وعلى نبينا وبقية الأنبياءِ الصَّلاةُ والسَّلامُ، وفي حاشية عبد الحكيم على القطبِ؛ اكتفى في شرح المطالع على الشَّبابِ، وَهُوَ ظَاهِرُ ، وأَمَّا الشَّيْبُ : بياضُ الشَّعرِ أو السِّنُّ الَّذي تَضعفُ فيهِ الحرارةُ الغريزيَّةُ، ففي كَوْنِهِ بطيء الزَّوالِ خفاءٌ، إِلَّا أَنْ يُرادَ بِهِ الشَّيْبُ الغيرُ الطَّبيعي، فإنَّهُ يزولُ بالأدوية بِمُدَّةٍ مَديدةٍ، وسَمِعْتُ أَنَّهُم يُعالجون بالمعاجين مُدَّةٌ مَديدةٌ ; فَيصيرُ الشَّعرُ الأبيضُ أسود، وتعودُ القوَّةُ الَّتي في الشَّبابِ، وكَتبُوهَا في كتبهم، ورأيْتُ شيخاً بلغَ عُمرُهُ مئةٌ وسِتّ عشرةَ سنةٌ قَدْ صَارَ شعرُ لِحيَتِهِ البيضاء مِن أصلِهِ أسودَ ، وبقي بياض في أعلاه يتبدَّلُ يوماً فيوماً بالسواد
Like permanent poverty, or it departs quickly like the redness of shyness and the yellowness of fear, or slowly like youth and gray hair. al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'like permanent poverty' means like permanent dependence on other than God. As for dependence on God, it is an inseparable accident. Then if you consider dependence on others with respect to a human, it is a general accident because it is said of him and of other types of animals. If you consider it with respect to animals, it is specific because it is not absent from them without other things. His statement: 'like the redness of shyness' means the redness that occurs at shyness, i.e., modesty, and the yellowness of fear, i.e., the yellowness occurring at fear, i.e., fright. His statement: 'like youth and gray hair' means old age, and its apparent meaning is that each of them departs slowly. As for the first, it is clear. As for the second, because gray hair departs with youth, as it is reported that al-Khidr... al-Atar: It is possible to cure them from separation of continuity and otherwise, and by the opposite what departs with the persistence of the subject; that was not reported. Abu al-Fath said that, and al-Muhaqqiq al-Razi answered in his Sharh al-Matali' that permanence may be free from necessity in particulars, but it is not free from it in universals. So it is permissible that a separable accident be permanently established for a particular one of its particulars while being separable from it. Al-Sayyid refuted him in his glosses because the necessary conjunction mentioned here is an expression of necessity in the broader sense, and there is no doubt that permanence is not separated from necessity in this sense absolutely, whether in the particular or the universal. The mentioned difference, assuming its completeness, is only in permanence with respect to the necessity arising from essence, as they said. Then he answered the original objection by saying that the division of the separable accident into permanent and transient is a rational division because the mind can conceive that what is not impossible to separate from the essence may be permanently established for it, because it is conceivable for permanence to be separate from necessity at first sight, even if it is not actually permissible. In the marginalia of Mir Zahid: Apparently this division is based on their saying: the permanent is absolutely more general than the necessary. So. His statement: 'like youth and gray hair' its apparent meaning is that each of them departs. As for the first, it is clear. As for the second, there is a consideration. It was answered that gray hair may depart with youth, as it is reported that al-Khidr after every one hundred and twenty years has passed on him, his old age departs and his youth returns to him; and as it is reported that Zulaykha returned to her youth when she married Yusuf, peace be upon him, according to the opinion that he married her. And some said: his statement 'like youth and gray hair' may mean 'like youth with gray hair,' for it departs with it, so the example is one. His statement: 'If it is said:' This question is raised against the author's statement, otherwise it is separable, then dividing it into being permanent or transient; apparently the division is not valid. al-Atar: ...peace be upon him, after one hundred and twenty years have passed on him, he returns to youth, and this single instance suffices for the correctness of the departure of gray hair with youth. Likewise what was reported that Zulaykha returned to her youth when she married Yusuf, peace be upon him and upon our Prophet and the rest of the prophets, prayers and peace. And in the marginalia of Abd al-Hakim on al-Qutb, he sufficed in Sharh al-Matali' with youth, and it is apparent. As for gray hair: the whiteness of hair or the age in which natural heat weakens, its being slow in departure is obscure, unless what is meant is unnatural gray hair, for it departs with medicine over a long period. I heard that they treat with pastes over a long period; then white hair becomes black, and the strength of youth returns, and they wrote it in their books. I saw an old man who reached one hundred and sixteen years of age, the white hair of his beard became black from its root, and whiteness remained at its top, changing day by day to black.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the division of the separable accident (al-'arad al-mufariq) into permanent (da'im) and transient (za'il), as found in the matn of Tadhheeb al-Mantiq. The commentator, al-Dasuqi, begins by elaborating on the examples given in the matn. First, he explains the example of 'permanent poverty' (al-faqr al-da'im), meaning dependence on others than God. He clarifies that dependence on God is not separable, so it is not an example. He then distinguishes between considering poverty relative to a human (making it a general accident applicable to animals) and relative to animals (making it specific). Next, he explains the examples of quickly transient accidents: the redness of shyness (humrat al-khajal) and the yellowness of fear (sufrat al-wajal), noting that these are temporary physiological responses. For slowly transient accidents, he gives youth (shabab) and gray hair (shayb). He notes that gray hair can depart with youth, citing the tradition of al-Khidr who regains youth every 120 years and the story of Zulaykha. At this point, a digression by al-Atar discusses medical treatments for gray hair. Following this, the text presents an objection (fa-in qila): 'How can a separable accident be permanent? If it were permanent, it would not be separable.' This is a logical problem because permanence seems to imply inseparability. The commentator al-Dasuqi then summarizes responses from earlier scholars. Al-Razi in Sharh al-Matali' answers that permanence may be free from necessity in particulars, but not in universals; thus a separable accident can be permanently attached to a particular instance. Al-Sayyid refutes this by arguing that the necessity in question is the broader kind, and permanence never separates from it in either particular or universal. Another response is that the division is purely rational: the mind can conceive of a separable accident being permanent even if it is not actually possible. The margin of Mir Zahid adds that the division is based on the principle that 'permanent' is more general than 'necessary.' The commentary then returns to the example of youth and gray hair, emphasizing that they are slow to depart but not permanent. The passage ends with a medical discussion of gray hair and a first-hand account of an old man whose beard turned black again.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, which of the following is an example of a slowly transient (ya‘zulu bi-but') accidental quality?
A) Permanent poverty (al-faqr al-da'im)
B) The redness of shyness (humrat al-khajal)
C) Youth (al-shabab)
D) Yellowing of fear (sufrat al-wajal)

Key Takeaway
The division of separable accidents into permanent and transient is based on rational possibility and illustrated with concrete examples.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the following are permanent or transient separable accidents: a) the heat of a fire (assuming the fire persists but can be removed). b) human anger (which comes and goes). Use the examples from the lesson.
?
Classify 'the ability to speak' in humans as permanent or transient, and justify using the logic of the division.

The Ten Categories
intermediate
5 min
Resolving the Objection: Can a Separable Accident Be Permanent?
Commentary addresses the objection that a separable accident cannot be permanent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection to permanent separable accidents and evaluate the responses of al-Razi and al-Sayyid.

Core Idea
The mind can conceive a separable accident as permanent; this rational division survives logical scrutiny.

An objection is raised: if an accident is truly separable, how can it be permanent? Permanence seems to imply inseparability. The commentary presents responses from major logicians such as al-Razi and al-Sayyid Sharif, showing how the logical community defended the division. This lesson walks through that debate and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection (fa-in qila)
The objection states: 'If the accident is separable, then dividing it into permanent and transient is apparently invalid, because permanence would contradict separability.' This is the core logical challenge.
The objection is taken as serious; the commentator does not dismiss it.
2
Analyze al-Razi's response
Al-Razi in Sharh al-Matali' answers that permanence may be free from necessity (darura) in particulars. So a particular instance of a separable accident can be permanent even though the universal type is separable. This allows a permanent separable accident for a specific subject.
This response uses the distinction between universal and particular.
3
Examine al-Sayyid's refutation
Al-Sayyid Sharif refutes al-Razi by arguing that the 'necessity' in question is the broader sense (al-darura bi al-ma'na al-a'mm), which includes permanence. Thus, permanence always implies necessity, whether in particulars or universals. So al-Razi's distinction fails.
This is a tighter logical point, showing that permanence entails necessity in the relevant sense.
4
Consider the alternative response
Another response given is that the division is purely rational (taqsim 'aqli). The mind can conceive of a separable accident being permanent, even if it is not actually possible. This is a defense based on mental consideration, not actual existence.
Mir Zahid supports this, noting that the division assumes 'permanent' is more general than 'necessary'.
5
Synthesize the outcome
The debate shows that the division is logically defensible: either by restricting to particulars (al-Razi, though refuted) or by appealing to rational division (the accepted view). The key is that 'permanent' does not equal 'necessary' in the strict sense.
The discussion refines the understanding of modal terms like permanence and necessity.

كَالْفَقْرِ الدَّائِمِ، أَوْ يَزُوْلَ عنه بِسُرْعَةٍ كحُمُرةِ الخجل، وصُفْرَةِ الوَجَلِ، أَوْ بُطْءٍ كالشَّبابِ ، والشَّيبِ . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : كَالْفَقْرِ الدَّائِم) أي : كَالافتقارِ لِغيرِ اللهِ الدَّائم، وأمَّا الافتقار إلى الله؛ فهو عرضُ لازم، ثمَّ إِنَّ الافتقار للغير إن اعتبرته بالنّسبة للإنسان؛ كان عرضاً عاما ؛ لأنَّهُ يُقالُ عليه وعلى غيره من أنواع الحيوان، وإن اعتبرته بالنسبة للحيوان؛ كانَ خاصا ؛ لأنَّهُ لَا يخلو عنه دون غيرِهِ. (قَوْلُهُ : كَالشَّبَابِ وَالشَّيْبِ أي : الهرم، وظاهرُهُ أَنَّ كُلا منهما يزولُ بعد بطء، أمَّا الأوّلُ؛ فَظاهر، وأمَّا الثَّاني؛ فَلِأنَّ الشَّيب يزولُ بالشَّبابِ كما وردّ أَنَّ الخَضِرَ العطار يُمكِنُ برُؤُهَا مِنْ تفرُّقِ الاتصال وغيره، وبالقابل ما يزولُ مع بقاء الموضوع، لم يرد ذلك ا.هـ. قاله أبو الفتح، وأجابَ المحقِّقُ الرَّازِيُّ في شَرْحِ المطالعِ بأنَّ الدَّوامَ قدْ يخلو عَن الضَّرورةِ في الجزئيَّاتِ، وإِنَّما لَا يَخلو عنها في الكُلِّيَّاتِ، فَيجوزُ أنْ يثبت عرضُ مُفارقٌ دائماً لِجزئي مِن جُزئيَّاتِهِ مَعَ انفكاكِهِ عنه، وَرَدَّهُ السَّيِّدُ في حَوَاشِيهِ؛ بأنَّ اللُّزوم المذكورَ هَهُنَا عبارةٌ عَن الضَّرورة بالمعنى الأعمّ، وَلَا شَكٌّ أَنَّ الدَّوامَ لا ينفكُ عَن الضَّرورةِ بِهَذا المعنى مطلقاً؛ سواء كانَ في الجزئي أو الكُلّيّ، والفرقُ المذكورُ على تقديرِ تَمَامِهِ؛ إِنَّما هو في الدَّوامِ بالقياس إلى الضَّرورةِ النَّاشِئَةِ عَنِ الذَّاتَ عَلَى مَا قالُوا ثم أجابَ عَنْ أصل الإشكالِ بأنَّ تقسيم العرض المفارق إلى الدائم والزَّائل تقسيم عقليّ ؛ لِتَجويز العقل أنْ يكونَ مَا لَا يمتنع انفكاكه عَنِ الماهية ثابتاً لها دائماً ؛ لِجَوازِ انفكاكِ الدَّوامِ عَنِ الضَّرورةِ في بادئ الرأي وإِنْ لم يكن جائِزَاً في نَفْسِ الأمر .ا.هـ. وفي حاشية مير زاهد : الظَّاهِرُ أنَّ هذا التقسيمَ مَبني على قولهم : الدَّائمةُ أعمُ مُطلقاً مِنَ الضَّروريَّةِ .ا.هـ. (قَوْلُهُ : كَالشَّبَابِ وَالشَّيْبِ ظاهرُهُ أَنَّ كُلَّا مِنْهُمَا يَزولُ، أَمَّا الْأَوَّلُ: فَوَاضِحُ، وأَمَّا الثَّاني: فَفِيهِ نَظَر، وأُجِيبُ بأنَّ الشَّيبَ قد يزولُ بِالشَّبابِ؛ كَمَا وردَ أَنَّ الخَضِرَ بعد مُضيّ كلّ مئةٍ وعشرين سنة عليه يزولُ هرمه ويعودُ له شبابه، وَكَما وردَ : أَنَّ زليخا رجعت إلى شبابها عند تزوج يوسفَ عليه السَّلامُ بها على القول بأنَّهُ تَزوَّجَهَا . وقال بعضُهم : قوله : كـ: «الشَّباب، والشَّيْب»؛ لعلَّ المرادَ كَـ: «الشَّبابِ معَ الشيب»، فإنَّهُ يزولُ بِهِ، فَالمثالُ واحد . (قَوْلُهُ : فَإِنْ قِيلَ : ( هذا السؤال وارد على قول المصنف، وإلا ، فَمُفارقٌ ، ثم تقسيمه إلى كونه يدوم أو يزول؛ فَبِحسَبِ الظَّاهِرِ لا يصحُ التَّقسيم العطار . عليهِ السَّلامُ بعدَ مُضيّ مئة وعشرين سنةٌ عليه يعود إلى الشَّبابِ، ويكفي هذا الفردُ لِصعَّةِ زوالِ الشَّيبِ بِالشَّبابِ، وكَذَا مَا ورد أنَّ زليخا رجعت إلى شبابها عند تزوج يوسف عليه وعلى نبينا وبقية الأنبياءِ الصَّلاةُ والسَّلامُ، وفي حاشية عبد الحكيم على القطبِ؛ اكتفى في شرح المطالع على الشَّبابِ، وَهُوَ ظَاهِرُ ، وأَمَّا الشَّيْبُ : بياضُ الشَّعرِ أو السِّنُّ الَّذي تَضعفُ فيهِ الحرارةُ الغريزيَّةُ، ففي كَوْنِهِ بطيء الزَّوالِ خفاءٌ، إِلَّا أَنْ يُرادَ بِهِ الشَّيْبُ الغيرُ الطَّبيعي، فإنَّهُ يزولُ بالأدوية بِمُدَّةٍ مَديدةٍ، وسَمِعْتُ أَنَّهُم يُعالجون بالمعاجين مُدَّةٌ مَديدةٌ ; فَيصيرُ الشَّعرُ الأبيضُ أسود، وتعودُ القوَّةُ الَّتي في الشَّبابِ، وكَتبُوهَا في كتبهم، ورأيْتُ شيخاً بلغَ عُمرُهُ مئةٌ وسِتّ عشرةَ سنةٌ قَدْ صَارَ شعرُ لِحيَتِهِ البيضاء مِن أصلِهِ أسودَ ، وبقي بياض في أعلاه يتبدَّلُ يوماً فيوماً بالسواد
Like permanent poverty, or it departs quickly like the redness of shyness and the yellowness of fear, or slowly like youth and gray hair. al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'like permanent poverty' means like permanent dependence on other than God. As for dependence on God, it is an inseparable accident. Then if you consider dependence on others with respect to a human, it is a general accident because it is said of him and of other types of animals. If you consider it with respect to animals, it is specific because it is not absent from them without other things. His statement: 'like the redness of shyness' means the redness that occurs at shyness, i.e., modesty, and the yellowness of fear, i.e., the yellowness occurring at fear, i.e., fright. His statement: 'like youth and gray hair' means old age, and its apparent meaning is that each of them departs slowly. As for the first, it is clear. As for the second, because gray hair departs with youth, as it is reported that al-Khidr... al-Atar: It is possible to cure them from separation of continuity and otherwise, and by the opposite what departs with the persistence of the subject; that was not reported. Abu al-Fath said that, and al-Muhaqqiq al-Razi answered in his Sharh al-Matali' that permanence may be free from necessity in particulars, but it is not free from it in universals. So it is permissible that a separable accident be permanently established for a particular one of its particulars while being separable from it. Al-Sayyid refuted him in his glosses because the necessary conjunction mentioned here is an expression of necessity in the broader sense, and there is no doubt that permanence is not separated from necessity in this sense absolutely, whether in the particular or the universal. The mentioned difference, assuming its completeness, is only in permanence with respect to the necessity arising from essence, as they said. Then he answered the original objection by saying that the division of the separable accident into permanent and transient is a rational division because the mind can conceive that what is not impossible to separate from the essence may be permanently established for it, because it is conceivable for permanence to be separate from necessity at first sight, even if it is not actually permissible. In the marginalia of Mir Zahid: Apparently this division is based on their saying: the permanent is absolutely more general than the necessary. So. His statement: 'like youth and gray hair' its apparent meaning is that each of them departs. As for the first, it is clear. As for the second, there is a consideration. It was answered that gray hair may depart with youth, as it is reported that al-Khidr after every one hundred and twenty years has passed on him, his old age departs and his youth returns to him; and as it is reported that Zulaykha returned to her youth when she married Yusuf, peace be upon him, according to the opinion that he married her. And some said: his statement 'like youth and gray hair' may mean 'like youth with gray hair,' for it departs with it, so the example is one. His statement: 'If it is said:' This question is raised against the author's statement, otherwise it is separable, then dividing it into being permanent or transient; apparently the division is not valid. al-Atar: ...peace be upon him, after one hundred and twenty years have passed on him, he returns to youth, and this single instance suffices for the correctness of the departure of gray hair with youth. Likewise what was reported that Zulaykha returned to her youth when she married Yusuf, peace be upon him and upon our Prophet and the rest of the prophets, prayers and peace. And in the marginalia of Abd al-Hakim on al-Qutb, he sufficed in Sharh al-Matali' with youth, and it is apparent. As for gray hair: the whiteness of hair or the age in which natural heat weakens, its being slow in departure is obscure, unless what is meant is unnatural gray hair, for it departs with medicine over a long period. I heard that they treat with pastes over a long period; then white hair becomes black, and the strength of youth returns, and they wrote it in their books. I saw an old man who reached one hundred and sixteen years of age, the white hair of his beard became black from its root, and whiteness remained at its top, changing day by day to black.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the division of the separable accident (al-'arad al-mufariq) into permanent (da'im) and transient (za'il), as found in the matn of Tadhheeb al-Mantiq. The commentator, al-Dasuqi, begins by elaborating on the examples given in the matn. First, he explains the example of 'permanent poverty' (al-faqr al-da'im), meaning dependence on others than God. He clarifies that dependence on God is not separable, so it is not an example. He then distinguishes between considering poverty relative to a human (making it a general accident applicable to animals) and relative to animals (making it specific). Next, he explains the examples of quickly transient accidents: the redness of shyness (humrat al-khajal) and the yellowness of fear (sufrat al-wajal), noting that these are temporary physiological responses. For slowly transient accidents, he gives youth (shabab) and gray hair (shayb). He notes that gray hair can depart with youth, citing the tradition of al-Khidr who regains youth every 120 years and the story of Zulaykha. At this point, a digression by al-Atar discusses medical treatments for gray hair. Following this, the text presents an objection (fa-in qila): 'How can a separable accident be permanent? If it were permanent, it would not be separable.' This is a logical problem because permanence seems to imply inseparability. The commentator al-Dasuqi then summarizes responses from earlier scholars. Al-Razi in Sharh al-Matali' answers that permanence may be free from necessity in particulars, but not in universals; thus a separable accident can be permanently attached to a particular instance. Al-Sayyid refutes this by arguing that the necessity in question is the broader kind, and permanence never separates from it in either particular or universal. Another response is that the division is purely rational: the mind can conceive of a separable accident being permanent even if it is not actually possible. The margin of Mir Zahid adds that the division is based on the principle that 'permanent' is more general than 'necessary.' The commentary then returns to the example of youth and gray hair, emphasizing that they are slow to depart but not permanent. The passage ends with a medical discussion of gray hair and a first-hand account of an old man whose beard turned black again.

Key Terms
العرض المفارق
دائم
زائل
ضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's response in the commentary, why can a separable accident be permanent?
A) Because permanence never implies necessity
B) Because in particulars, permanence may be free from necessity
C) Because the division is empirical
D) Because all accidents are permanent

Key Takeaway
The division of separable accidents into permanent and transient is a rational division, not contradicted by the fact that permanence often implies necessity in a broader sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

In a debate, your opponent argues: 'Knowledge in the Prophet (peace be upon him) is an accident, but it is permanent, so it cannot be separable.' Use the responses from this lesson to counter.

Dispute on the Existence of the Natural Universal
Author presents and resolves the dispute on whether the natural universal has dependent existence in external individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio ad absurdum argument against the dependent existence of the natural universal and identify the logical conditional used.

Core Idea
The natural universal exists only in the mind, not externally even as a part.

In this passage, the author tackles a deep metaphysical question: does the natural universal, like 'animal', have any kind of existence in the external world? While everyone agrees it does not exist independently, a dispute arises over whether it exists dependently as a part of each particular. The author rejects this with a powerful logical argument. Let's walk through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the agreed point
All parties agree that the natural universal has no independent existence externally, because only particulars exist externally.
This is the starting premise of the discussion.
2
Locate the dispute
The disagreement is whether the universal has dependent existence as a part of its individuals. Some scholars affirm this, others deny it entirely.
The commentator is said to follow the affirmative view.
3
Extract the reductio premise
The critics assume for argument that the universal is a real part of the particular. If so, then as a part it would exist wherever the particular exists.
This is the antecedent of the conditional.
4
Follow the conditional chain
If the universal is a part, it would have to be in multiple places simultaneously (e.g., Zayd and Bakr) and possess contradictory attributes (e.g., black and white, alive and dead). This is impossible.
The consequent is impossible, so by modus tollens the antecedent is false.
5
Recognize the conclusion
Therefore, the universal cannot be a part, and the natural universal is merely a mental consideration with no external existence at all.
This resolves the dispute in favor of the critics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل : أنَّ الكُلِّيَّ الطَّبيعي لا وجود له في الخارج استقلالاً باتفاق؛ لأنَّ الموجود في الخارج لا يكون إلا جزئيا . واختلف؛ هَل له وجود في خارج الأعيان في ضمن أفرادِهِ؛ فيكون وجوده في الخارج تبعاً؛ لأنَّهُ جزءٌ لِلأفراد الموجودة، وجزء الموجود موجود؟، وهذا قول جماعة، وتبعهُمُ الشَّارح . وذهب آخرون إلى أنَّ الكُلِّيَّ الطَّبيعي لا وجود له؛ لا استقلالاً وَلَا تبعاً، واختاره بعضُ المحققين قائلاً : لَا تُسلَّمُ أنَّ الكُلّيّ جزء للجزئي الموجود في الخارج ؛ إذْ لَو كانَ جزءاً له؛ لَلَزِمَ أنْ يحلَّ الشَّيءُ الواحد في أمكنةٍ مُتعدّدةٍ في آن واحد؛ لأنَّ الحيوانَ الكُلِّيَّ مُتحقِّق في زيدٍ وبكر المختلفي المكان والأوصاف، فيلزمُ أَنَّهُ موجود في المشرقِ والمغرب، وأنَّهُ أسود وأبيض، وطويل وقصير، وحي وميت، وهذا باطل، فَلِذا كانَ التَّحقيقُ : أنَّ الكُلّي الطَّبيعي أمر اعتباري لا وجود له خارجاً أصلاً، والموجود في الخارج جزئيات على صورةِ الكُلّيّ المرتسمة في العقلِ.
The summary: The natural universal has no independent existence in the external world by agreement, because what exists externally is only particular. And there is disagreement: Does it have existence in external entities within its individuals, such that its existence is dependent because it is a part of existing individuals, and the part of the existent is existent? This is the view of a group, and the commentator followed them. And others went that the natural universal has no existence, neither independent nor dependent, and some investigators chose this, saying: It is not granted that the universal is a part of the particular existing externally; for if it were a part of it, it would necessitate that one thing subsist in multiple places at one time, because the universal animal is realized in Zayd and Bakr who differ in place and attributes, so it would follow that it exists in the east and the west, and that it is black and white, and tall and short, and alive and dead, and this is false. Therefore the verification is: The natural universal is a mental consideration with no existence externally at all, and what exists externally are particulars upon the form of the universal depicted in the mind.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of the ontological status of the natural universal (al-kulli al-tabi'i). The author begins by stating an agreed point: the universal has no independent external existence, since only particulars exist externally. He then presents a dispute: some scholars, including the commentator, hold that the universal exists dependently within its individuals as a part of them. Others deny any external existence at all. The author endorses the latter view and provides a reductio ad absurdum: if the universal 'animal' were a real part of each individual, it would have to be in multiple places at once with contradictory attributes, which is impossible. Therefore, the universal is a mental consideration. The argument uses the example of Zayd and Bakr sharing the essence 'animal' but differing in location and accidents. This mirrors the logical principle that the universal as such cannot be instantiated in multiple particulars without contradiction unless it is merely conceptual. In Islamic logic, this debate is fundamental for understanding how universals relate to particulars, especially in discussions of essence and existence.

Key Terms
الكلي الطبيعي (al-kulli al-tabi'i): The natural universal, i.e., the essence considered in itself without any addition, which is neither universal nor particular in its own nature but is considered as predicated of many. [Note: This term is stable across the tradition, though some later logicians distinguish it from the logical and mental universals.]
وجود استقلالي (wujud istiqlali): Independent existence, i.e., the universal existing by itself as a separate entity, which is denied here.
وجود تبعي (wujud tab'i): Dependent existence, i.e., the universal existing as a part of an external particular, which is the disputed point.
أمر اعتباري (amr i'tibari): A mental consideration, something that exists only in the mind, not externally. [Note: In later philosophy, this term can also refer to relational concepts; here it means a purely conceptual existence.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the conclusive position on the natural universal according to the author?
A) It exists independently externally.
B) It exists dependently as a part of individuals.
C) It is a mental consideration with no external existence.
D) It exists only in the divine mind.

Key Takeaway
The natural universal is an abstraction that exists only in the mind; any attempt to give it external existence leads to logical absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a conditional argument (modus tollens) using the dispute about the 'illah in usul al-fiqh: if the 'illah were a real entity in each individual, then it would have to be in multiple places simultaneously; but that is impossible; therefore the 'illah is a mental consideration.
?
Identify whether the following argument uses a similar reductio: 'If the universal 'knowledge' were a part of each knower, then contradictory knowledges would coexist; but that is impossible; therefore knowledge is not a part.'

The Priority of Verbal Definition Among Conceptual Questions
Debate on the classification of verbal definition as a conceptual question.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish verbal definition from real definition and explain why verbal definition is classified as a conceptual (tasawwuri) rather than assential (tasdiqi) question.

Core Idea
Verbal definition merely brings a stored image to mind; it is a conceptual tool, not a real inquiry into essence.

This lesson unpacks a debate about whether verbal definition (al-ta'rīf al-lafẓī) belongs to the explanatory 'what' question (mā al-shāriḥa) and how it relates to real definitions and existence questions. Drawing on commentaries by al-Dawwānī, Abū al-Fatḥ, and others, the passage clarifies that verbal definition is a conceptual (taṣawwurī) inquiry because it retrieves an already stored meaning rather than establishing a new essence. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the order of questions in Islamic logical inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
Is verbal definition part of conceptual or assential questions? The passage argues it is conceptual because it only retrieves a stored image.
2
Understand the classification of questions
The passage discusses four types: nominal 'what' (mā al-ismiyya), real 'what' (mā al-ḥaqīqiyya), simple 'whether' (hal al-basīṭa), and compound 'whether' (hal al-murakkaba). Verbal definition is linked to nominal 'what'.
3
Follow al-Dawwānī's reasoning
Al-Dawwānī argues that verbal definition belongs to nominal 'what' because its purpose is mental representation (taṣwīr). It brings a stored image to mind, so it is a conceptual question.
4
Analyze Abū al-Fatḥ's objection
Abū al-Fatḥ objects that nominal definition is not limited to verbal definition; there are other forms. However, the priority of nominal 'what' does not depend on this, as any conceptual definition suffices.
5
Synthesize the final position
Despite the objection, verbal definition is still classified as a conceptual question because it meets the criterion of bringing a stored image to mind, which is the lowest level of conception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مُقدَّم على مطلبِ مَا الحقيقيَّةِ ؛ إذْ مَا لم يعلم وجودُ الشَّيء؛ لم يكنْ أنْ يتصوَّرَ مِن حيثُ إِنَّهُ مَوجودٌ، وَلَا يكونُ التَّرتيبُ ضَروريًّا بينَ الهَليَّةِ المركبة والمائية الحقيقية، لكنَّ الأَوْلَى تقديم المائية .ا.هـ. وفي شرح سُلَّم العلوم: مطلب «ما» الشَّارحةِ مُتقدِّم على «ما» الحقيقية وجوباً؛ إذْ لَمَّا لم يصدّق بالوجوب ؛ كيف يطلب الحقيقة؟ وعلى المركَّبةِ استحساناً؛ بناءً على أنَّ لا كمالَ بعلم أحوال المعدوماتِ ومشكوك الوجود، وتقديمُ مَا الحقيقيَّةِ على هَل المركبة استحساني ؛ إِذِ الأَحَرَى مَعرفتُهُ للكنه أوَّلاً ، ثم العوارض، ثمَّ إِنَّ الأنسب لمجيبِ السَّائلِ بِـ «ما» الشارحة؛ الجواب بالحدّ ليستغنى عن «مَا الحقيقيَّةِ ، كَمَا إذا سُئِلَ : مَا الزَّمان؟ فالجواب: الحش كم مُتَّصل غير قارٍ، لا أنَّه عدد الحركة، وللسائل بـ «لم»؛ الجوابُ بِاللّمِّيِّ لئلا يحتاج إلى سؤالِ اللّمّ بعدَهُ .ا.هـ. قال مير زاهد وتسمية إحدى الهليتين بالبسيطة والأُخرى بالمركَّبةِ؛ إنَّما هو بالنَّظر إلى ما صدقهما ؛ لا إلى مفهوم القضيَّةِ المقصودة، فإنَّ مصداق الهليَّةِ البسيطة هو نفس الموضوع من حيثُ يصح انتزاع وصفِ الوجودِ عنه، ومصداق الهلّيَّةِ المركَّبة هو الموضوعُ مَعَ شيءٍ آخر .ا.هـ. ومعنى كلام الدَّوَّاني ؛ أَنَّهُ علَّلَ القومُ ... إلخ ؛ أي: يدلّ على أنَّ الغرض منهُ التَّصويرُ ، تعليلُ القوم تقدُّمُ مَا » الاسميَّةِ ؛ أي : الشَّارحة لمعنى الاسم على بقيَّةِ المطالب، وهو داخل تحت مطلبِ (ما)) الاسميَّةِ، فيكونُ تَعريفاً لفظيًّا، إذ لو كان تعريفاً حقيقيًّا؛ لدخل تحت مطلبِ ما الحقيقية، فتعليلُ القوم إنَّما يتم إذا كانَ التَّعريف اللفظي داخلاً تحت مطلب ما ، لأنَّ فهم المعنى مِنَ اللَّفْظِ يحصلُ مِنَ التَّعريف اللفظي، كما أنَّه يحصل من التّعريف الاسميّ، فلو لم يكن اللفظي داخلاً في مطلب «ما» كما أنَّ الاسمي داخل فيه ؛ لم يكن هذا المطلب مُتقدِّماً على سائر المطالب، ولم يصح احتياجها إليه، قال أبو الفتح: : وَمَا ذكرَهُ إِنَّما يتم إذا لم يكن لمطلب ( ما ) الاسميَّةِ صورةٌ غيرُ التَّعريف اللفظي، وهو ممنوع ، بل الظاهرُ أنَّ التَّعريفات الاسميَّةً داخلة في مطلب ما الاسميَّةِ اتفاقاً، وَمِنَ البيِّنِ أَنَّهُ يكفي لتقدُّم هذا المطلب على سائر المطالب تقدُّمُ التَّصورِ الحاصلِ بالتَّعريف الاسمي عليها ؛ سواء كانَ التَّعريفُ اللَّفظِيُّ مِنَ المطالبِ التَّصوريَّة أو التصديقيَّةِ .ا.هـ. ثمَّ قالَ الدَّوَّانِيُّ : والتَّفصيلُ أنَّ للتَّصورِ مراتب، أدناها ؛ أن يستحضر في المدركة صورةً مخزونةً بواسطة لفظ موضوع بإزائِهِ، فإنْ حصل ذلك ابتداء؛ فَلَا يتصوَّرُ طلبٌ كَمَا إذا أُطلِقَ اللَّفظ الموضوع بإزاء معنى بالنسبة إلى العالم بالوضعِ ؛ فَفَهِمَ مَعناه، وهذا لا يدخل في سلسلة المطالب لعدم الطَّلبِ، وإنْ حصل بعد إلقاء لفظ لم يعرف معناه؛ فهناكَ يتصوَّرُ الطَّلب، كما إذا قيلَ: الخلاءُ مُحالٌ ; فيقالُ : ما الخلاءُ؟ فيُجابُ بأَنَّهُ بُعد موهوم، فهذا تعريفٌ لفظي، والغرضُ منهُ إحضار صورة مخزونةٍ، وهو بمنزلةِ التَّصورِ ابتداء، إلَّا أَنَّهُ من حيثُ إِنَّهُ مَسوقٌ بلفظ لم يُفهَمْ مَعناهُ بخصوصِهِ فيصحُ طلبُهُ ؛ عُدَّ مِنَ مَطلبِ مَا ، وأعلاهَا أنْ تحصلَ صورةٌ غيرُ حاصلةٍ في الخزانة، وفيه مراتب متفاوتة، وأتمها تصور الكنه، وذلك بالحدِّ التَّامّ فالتَّعريفُ اللفظي داخل في المطالب النَّصوريَّةِ ا.هـ. فقوله : والغرضُ ... إلخ؛ فيه إشارة إلى أنَّ التَّعريف اللفظي يحصِّلُهُ الإنسانُ لغيره لا لِنَفْسِهِ، وإلا ; يلزم تحصيل الحاصل، فإنَّ قصد إحضارِ الشَّيء لا يتصوَّرُ بدونِ حضوره، وإِنَّما كانَ الاستحسانُ أعلى التَّصورات والاستحضار أدناها ; لأنَّ الحصول في المدركة والخزانة حاصل باستحصال والحصول في المدركة فقط ;
Precedes the question of 'what is it in reality'; because if the existence of the thing is not known, it cannot be conceived from the aspect that it exists, and the ordering between the compound 'whether it is' and the real 'what' is not necessary, but it is preferable to present the 'what'. End. In the commentary of Sullam al-'Ulum: The question 'what' explanatory is necessarily prior to 'what' real; because if one does not affirm existence, how can he ask for the essence? And to the compound 'whether it is' it is preferable, based on the fact that there is no perfection in knowing the states of non-existents and doubtful existence. And presenting real 'what' before compound 'whether' is recommendable; since it is more appropriate to know the essence first, then the accidents. Then indeed the most suitable for one answering the asker with 'what' explanatory is to answer with a definition (hadd) so that he is independent of 'what' real, as when it is asked: What is time? So the answer: It is a continuous non-fixed extension, not that it is the number of motion. And for the asker with 'why', the answer is with the 'why' explanation to avoid needing the 'why' question after it. End. Mir Zahid said: The naming of one of the two 'whether' as simple and the other as compound is only with respect to their truth-bearers, not to the meaning of the intended proposition. For the truth-bearer of the simple 'whether' is the subject itself from which the attribute of existence may be extracted, and the truth-bearer of the compound 'whether' is the subject together with something else. End. And the meaning of al-Dawwani's statement: He said that the people's reasoning... etc.; i.e., it indicates that the purpose of it is mental representation, the people's reasoning for the priority of the nominal 'what' i.e., the explanatory of the meaning of the name over the other questions, and it is included under the question of nominal 'what', so it becomes a verbal definition, because if it were a real definition, it would be included under the question of real 'what'. So the people's reasoning is only valid if verbal definition is included under the question of 'what', because understanding the meaning from the word is obtained from verbal definition, just as it is obtained from nominal definition. So if verbal definition were not included in the question of 'what' as nominal definition is included, then this question would not be prior to the other questions, and their need for it would not be correct. Abu al-Fath said: What he mentioned is only valid if the question of nominal 'what' has no form other than verbal definition, which is denied. Rather it is apparent that nominal definitions are included in the question of nominal 'what' by agreement. And it is clear that it suffices for the priority of this question over other questions the priority of the concept obtained from nominal definition over them, regardless of whether verbal definition is among conceptual or assential questions. End. Then al-Dawwani said: The detail is that concept has levels. The lowest: to bring to mind a stored image by means of a word placed for it. If that occurs initially, then no request is conceived, as when a word placed for a meaning is uttered to one who knows the convention, and he understands its meaning. This does not enter the series of questions due to lack of request. And if it occurs after uttering a word whose meaning is not known, then a request is conceived, as when it is said: 'Void is impossible', and it is asked: What is void? So it is answered: It is an imaginary dimension. This is a verbal definition. Its purpose is to bring a stored image to mind. It is like initial conception, except that because it is prompted by a word whose specific meaning is not understood, a request for it is valid; it is counted among the question of 'what'. The highest level is that a form not already in the store is obtained, and it has varying levels. The most complete is conception of the essence (kunh), and that is by the perfect definition (hadd). So verbal definition is included among the conceptual questions. End. So his statement: 'and the purpose... etc.' indicates that verbal definition is acquired by one for another, not for himself, otherwise it would involve acquiring what is already obtained. For the intention to bring something to mind cannot be conceived without its presence. And indeed the most excellent is the highest level of concepts, and the lowest is bringing to mind ; because obtaining in both the perceptive faculty and the store is through investigation, while obtaining in the perceptive faculty only ;
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage is a sophisticated discussion about the place of verbal definition among the categories of questions in Islamic logic. The author, through various commentators, debates whether verbal definition falls under the explanatory 'what' (ma sharhah) and how it relates to real 'what' (ma haqiqiyya) and the 'whether' questions (hal basita and hal murakkaba). The first point: al-Attar argues that the explanatory 'what' is necessarily prior to real 'what' because existence must be known before essence. However, priority between compound 'whether' and real 'what' is not necessary but preferable. Then a quotation from Sharh Sullam al-'Ulum explains that the explanatory 'what' is necessarily prior to real 'what', and to compound 'whether' it is preferable. Then Mir Zahid clarifies the naming of simple and compound 'whether'. Then al-Dawwani is mentioned, and his reasoning that the purpose of verbal definition is mental representation (taswir), hence it belongs to the nominal 'what' question, making it a conceptual matter. Abu al-Fath objects that al-Dawwani's argument only holds if nominal 'what' has no other form than verbal definition, which is not the case. Al-Dawwani then offers a detailed analysis of levels of conception, showing that verbal definition is for bringing a stored image to mind, thus it is included among conceptual questions. The passage ends with a note that verbal definition is for others, not for oneself.

Checkpoint
According to al-Dawwānī, why is verbal definition included among conceptual questions?
A) It produces new knowledge about essence.
B) It only brings a stored image to mind.
C) It is a form of assent.
D) It is necessary for existence questions.

Key Takeaway
Verbal definition is a conceptual inquiry because it retrieves a pre-existing image; it does not establish a new reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Explain whether the question "What is the world?" asked by a mutakallim is a nominal or real 'what' question, using the criteria from this lesson.
?
Evaluate the following: When asked "What is time?", the response "It is a continuous non-fixed extension" is a verbal definition. Explain why this is classified as a conceptual question based on al-Dawwānī's analysis.

The Ten Categories
advanced
15 min
Exclusion of Natural Propositions and Ibn Sina’s Tripartite Division
Super-commentators explain why natural propositions are excluded and Ibn Sina’s tripartite division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why natural propositions are excluded from the philosophical sciences and describe Ibn Sina’s tripartite division of categorical propositions along with its justification.

Core Idea
Natural propositions judge the nature itself, but nature has no extramental existence, so they are excluded; Ibn Sina’s division has only three types.

The philosophical sciences investigate actually existing things, which are individuals. Natures exist only within individuals. Therefore, propositions that predicate solely of the nature lack a real subject and are not used. The super-commentators clarify this and show how Ibn Sina’s division of categoricals into personal, quantified, and indefinite reflects this exclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about natural propositions
Find in the Arabic the statement that natural propositions are not considered in the sciences. What is the justification given?
The justification is that nature has no external existence.
2
Understand the metaphysical basis
According to the super-commentators, why is nature not considered existent? Distinguish between the view of later scholars (natures are abstract) and earlier ones (natures exist within individuals).
Either way, the nature alone is not an independent existent.
3
Compare natural with personal propositions
Why are personal propositions not also excluded? Note the example of the first figure syllogism and the explanation that personal propositions can function as universal major premises.
Al-Sayyid’s answer: personal are considered within quantified ones; natural are not.
4
Understand Ibn Sina’s tripartite division
How did Ibn Sina divide categorical propositions? What three types did he include and why did he exclude the natural? Note the linguistic debate about the word ‘thalatha’ and the alternative views that try to include natural under personal or indefinite.
The division is personal, quantified, indefinite.
5
Evaluate the reasoning
Check the logic: if natural propositions have no extramental referent, they cannot serve as premises in syllogisms about real existents. This justifies their exclusion from the sciences.
