ثمَّ القضايا الطَّبيعية غير معتبرة في العلوم.
الدسوقي: ويمكنُ أنْ يكونَ الشَّارحُ أشار بقولِهِ : بأنْ لَا يُراد ... إلخ) إلى ذلك، لكن كانَ عليهِ أن يعينه بالمثالِ الَّذي لا يشمله ظاهرُ العبارة.
أصالة (قَوْلُهُ: غَيْرُ مُعْتَبَرةٍ فِي الْعُلُوم) أي : لأنَّ الطَّبيعةَ لا وجود لها في الخارج حتَّى يُحكم عليها أو بها ؛ أي لأنَّ الموجودات المتأصلة الأفراد، والطَّبيعة هي إنَّما توجد في ضمنها.
العطار: (قَوْلُهُ: ثُمَّ القَضَايَا الطَّبِيعِيَّةُ غَيْرُ مُعْتَبَرَةٍ فِي الْعُلُوم) «أل» عهديَّةٌ، والمراد العلوم الحكميَّةُ ; لأنَّ مسائِلَها قوانينُ كُلِّيَّةٌ، فلا بُدَّ مِنَ اعتبار انطباقها على جزئيات موضوعِهَا ، ولأنَّ بحث الحكمة عن الموجوداتِ والمتأصل في الوجود هو الأفراد ; لأنَّها هِى التى يترتّب عليها الآثارُ خَارجاً ، والطَّبائعُ إنَّما توجد في ضمنها، بمعنى أنَّها أمورٌ انتزاعيَّةٌ على ما هو رأي المتأخّرينَ النَّافِينَ لوجودِ الطَّبائع، أو بمعنى أنَّها لا توجد بدونِ الفردِ عند القائل بوجودِهَا وانضمام التَّشخُصاتِ إليها على ما سبق تحقيقُهُ، فَالمقصودُ مِنَ العلوم الحكميَّةِ : معرفةُ أحوال الموجوداتِ المتأصلة في الوجودِ، فإِنْ قُلْتَ: الشَّخصيَّةُ أيضاً غيرُ مُعتبرة في العلوم; إذْ لا يُبحث فيها عن الأشخاص، وأجابَ السَّيِّدُ بأنَّها مُعتبرةٌ في ضمن المحصوراتِ بخلافِ الطَّبيعةِ ; فإنَّها ليسَتْ مُعتبرةً لا في ذاتها ولا في ضمن المحصوراتِ; لأنَّ الحكم فيها على الأفرادِ لا على الطَّبائع، وأيضاً الشَّخصيَّةُ قد تقومُ في الظَّاهِرِ مقامَ الكُلِّيَّةِ، فتقعُ كُبرى الشَّكل الأوَّلِ ; نحو: هذا زيد وزيد حيوان; فهذا حيوان بخلافِ الطَّبيعية، فإِنَّها لا تنتج في كُبرى الشَّكلِ الأَوَّلِ كَقولِكَ : زيد إنسان، والإنسانُ نوع مع أَنَّهُ لا يصدق زيد نوع. ا.هـ وإنَّما قال في الظاهر بناءً على ما سبقَ لَهُ مِنَ التَّحقيقِ مِن أَنَّ الجزئي لا يحمل، وأنَّ معنى قولنا زيد إنسانُ : المسمَّى بِزيدٍ، فالكُبرى في الحقيقةِ كُلِّيَّةٌ، وأمَّا على ما حقَّقَهُ الدَّوَانيُّ مِن صحة حملِهِ; فَالشَّخصيَّةُ تقعُ كُبرى الشَّكلِ الأوَّلِ حقيقةٌ، كَمَا قالَه عبد الحكيم، وإنَّما خُصَّ الكلامُ بالكُبرى; لأنَّ الطَّبيعيَّةً تقعُ صُغرى الشَّكل الأوَّلِ، والصُّغرى لا اختصاص لَهَا بالعلوم حتَّى تكونَ مُناسبتها موجبة للاعتبار في العلوم، وأمَّا مَا يقومُ مقامَ الكُلِّيَّةِ; فَلَهُ مناسبةٌ تامَّةٌ بِمسائل العلوم; لأنَّها كُبرياتُ الشَّكلِ الأَوَّلِ. قالَ العصامُ : والمنطق خارج عَنِ الحكمة، فَلَا يردُ أنَّ قولَنا : كُلُّ جنس موصل بعيد وأمثاله، وقولُهُ: كُلُّ مُعرَّفٍ يجب أن يكونَ أجلى مِنَ المعرفِ من مسائل المنطقِ، فقد اعتبرَتِ الطَّبيعيَّاتُ كَالشَّخصيَّات، ثمَّ قالَ : بقي أنَّ مِن مسائلِ العِلْمِ الإلهي; أنَّ الكُلِّيَّ الطَّبيعي موجود، والفروع المندرجة فيها طبيعيات ... هـ. وردَّهُ عبد الحكيم بأنَّ الحكم في قولهم : الكُلِّي الطَّبيعي موجودٌ على الطَّبائع من حيثُ إنَّها أفراد للموضوع; لَا مِن حيثُ إِنَّها طبائع، وما قِيلَ : إنّ الحكم فيها على الطبيعة; فَوَهُمْ. (قَوْلُهُ : ثَلَّثَ الْقِسْمَةَ) قالَ العصامُ : استعمالُ ثَلَّثَ بهذا المعنى ; جراءةٌ في اللُّغةِ لا يرضَى بِهِ أهلُ النّقةِ، هذا ويتبادرُ منهُ أنَّهُ كانَ قبلَ الشَّيخِ التَّقسيم الرُّباعِيُّ ; فثلَّثَهُ الشَّيخُ ، ورده عبد الحكيم بأنَّهُ مُستعمل في اللغة وليسَ مُستحدثاً، وأنَّه لا يقتضي سابقيَّةً حالة . (قَوْلُهُ : وَحَصَرَهَا فِي الشَّخْصِيَّةِ) أي : جعلَهَا لا تخرج عن واحدةٍ مِنَ الثَّلاثةِ، فَبعضُهُم تكلَّفَ وأدرجها في الشَّخصيَّةِ بناءً على أنَّ الطَّبيعية لا تحتمل الشركة، وبعضُهم في المهملة بناءً على أنَّ معنى المهملة ما لم يبيَّنُ فيها كميَّةُ الأفراد ; سواء صلح الحكم عليها أوْ لَا ، ذكرَ هَدَيْنِ القَولَيْنِ في شرح المطالع، وأطال في ذلك
Then natural propositions are not considered in the sciences. al-Dasuqi: It is possible that the commentator indicated by his statement 'that is not intended... etc.' to that, but he should have specified it with an example not covered by the apparent wording. Asalah (his statement: 'not considered in the sciences') i.e., because nature has no existence in the external so that a judgment could be made upon it or by it; i.e., because the things that actually exist are individuals, and nature only exists within them. al-Attar: (His statement: 'Then natural propositions are not considered in the sciences') the 'al' is specific, and by 'sciences' is meant the philosophical sciences; because their issues are universal laws, so it is necessary to consider their applicability to the particulars of their subject, and because the investigation of philosophy is about existents, and that which is fundamental in existence is individuals, because they are the ones upon which effects follow externally, and natures only exist within them, meaning that they are abstractive matters according to the view of the later scholars who deny the existence of natures, or meaning that they do not exist without the individual according to those who affirm their existence and the addition of individuators to them as previously investigated. So the purpose of the philosophical sciences is knowledge of the states of existents that are fundamental in existence. If you say: personal propositions are also not considered in the sciences, since individuals are not investigated in them, the Sayyid answered that they are considered within the quantified ones, unlike the natural; for it is not considered in itself nor within the quantified ones, because the judgment in it is about individuals, not about natures. Also the personal proposition may apparently stand in the place of the universal, thus it occurs as the major premise of the first figure, e.g., 'This is Zayd' and 'Zayd is an animal' yields 'This is an animal', unlike the natural, for it does not produce a conclusion as the major premise of the first figure, like your saying 'Zayd is a human' and 'Human is a species' but it is not true that Zayd is a species. End of quote. And he said 'apparently' based on his previous investigation that the particular is not predicated, and that the meaning of our saying 'Zayd is a human' is 'the one named Zayd', so the major premise in reality is universal. But according to what al-Dawwani verified regarding the validity of its predication, the personal proposition actually occurs as the major premise of the first figure, as said by Abd al-Hakim. The discussion was specifically about the major premise because the natural proposition occurs as the minor premise of the first figure, and the minor premise has no specific connection to the sciences such that its suitability would necessitate it being considered in the sciences. As for what stands in the place of the universal, it has a complete suitability to the issues of the sciences because they are the major premises of the first figure. al-Isam said: Logic is outside philosophy, so it is not objected that our saying 'Every genus is a remote mediator' and the like, and his saying 'Every definiendum must be more evident than the definiens' are among the issues of logic, and natural propositions have been considered like personal propositions. Then he said: It remains that among the issues of theology is that the natural universal exists, and the branches subsumed under it are natural... etc. And Abd al-Hakim refuted him by saying that the judgment in their statement 'the natural universal exists' is about the natures inasmuch as they are individuals of the subject, not inasmuch as they are natures, and what was said that the judgment is upon nature is a mistake. (His statement: 'he divided into three') al-Isam said: The use of 'thalatha' in this sense is a violation of language that the scholars of language would not approve of. Also it implies that before the Shaykh there was a quadruple division, and the Shaykh made it triple. Abd al-Hakim refuted him by saying that it is used in the language and is not an innovation, and that it does not imply a prior state. (His statement: 'and he confined them to the personal, quantified, and indefinite') i.e., he made them not go beyond these three. Some strained and included it in the personal on the basis that the natural does not admit sharing, and some in the indefinite on the basis that the meaning of indefinite is that which is not specified with any quantity of individuals, whether the judgment is suitable or not. The two views have been mentioned in Sharh al-Mutali, and he expounded on that.
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) by al-Dasuqi and al-Attar on the commentary (sharh) of Tadhheeb Sharh. It addresses the status of natural propositions (قضايا طبيعية) in the philosophical sciences. The core claim is that natural propositions are not considered in the sciences. The commentators explain that this is because nature as such has no independent existence in the external world; only individual instantiations exist. Natures are either abstractive concepts (according to the later scholars who deny their reality) or they exist only within individuals (for those who affirm them). Therefore, the sciences, which aim to know the states of actually existing things, cannot use propositions that predicate of the nature itself, since there is no referent for that judgment.

The passage then anticipates an objection: if natural propositions are excluded, why are personal propositions (singular) also not excluded? They answer that personal propositions are considered within the quantified ones (i.e., when they are used as major premises in the first figure they function like universals). For example, 'This is Zayd, and Zayd is an animal' yields 'This is an animal.' The natural proposition, however, cannot serve as a major premise in the first figure; e.g., 'Zayd is a human, and Human is a species' does not yield 'Zayd is a species.' This is because the judgment in natural propositions is about the nature, not the individual.

The discussion then turns to Ibn Sina's practice in the Shifa, where he divided categorical propositions into three types: personal (شخصية), quantified (محصورة), and indefinite (مهملة), omitting the natural. The commentators justify this by noting that natural propositions are not used in the sciences. There follows a linguistic debate: some object to the word 'thalatha' (to divide into three) as a linguistic innovation, but Abd al-Hakim defends its usage. Finally, there is a note that some scholars have strained to include natural propositions either under personal or indefinite, but these are forced interpretations.

Throughout, the commentary is concerned with the epistemological foundation of the classification of propositions: only those that can refer to actual individuals or stand as universal premises are relevant to the sciences.

Key Terms
القضية الطبيعية
القضية الشخصية
المحصورة
المهملة
الشكل الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Natural propositions are excluded from the philosophical sciences because their subject is a nature that does not exist independently; therefore Ibn Sina’s tripartite division of categoricals omits them, allowing only propositions that can refer to individuals or quantifications thereof.

The Modified Proposition: Definition and Distinction
Defines the modified proposition and its distinction from the simple negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a modified proposition (ma'dula) and distinguish it from a simple negative proposition (saliba) based on the role of the negation particle.

Core Idea
In a modified proposition, negation becomes part of the predicate, not a relation-cutter.

The author divides categorical propositions into types, but the key insight is how the negation particle is used. In a modified proposition, the particle is integrated into the predicate, whereas in a simple negative, it negates the whole relation. This distinction is essential for understanding propositional logic in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three-way division
Locate in the Arabic where the author says categorical propositions are divided into modified (ma'dula), simple? (muhassala), and simple? (basita). Note that the exact terms may vary, but the focus is on the modified type.
The passage mentions 'تقسيم الحملية إلى معدولة ومحصلة وبسيطة'.
2
Extract the defining feature of the modified proposition
The matn states: 'وَقَدْ يُجْعَلُ حَرْفُ السَّلْبِ ... جُزْءاً مِنْ جُزْءٍ' meaning the negation particle is made part of the predicate. This is the core characteristic.
'جزءاً من جزء' likely means the particle is part of the predicate (جزء of the predicate which itself is a جزء of the proposition).
3
Differentiate from the simple negative
Attar explains that in a simple negative (saliba), the negation particle cuts off the relation (قاطع للنسبة) and is not part of the predicate. In a modified proposition, it is integrated. Observe how this distinction prevents the definition from being too broad.
The simple negative is like 'Zayd is not standing' (negation of the relation), while the modified is like 'Zayd is not-standing' (negation as part of the subject's attribute).
4
Test the definition against Attar's objection
Attar raises that if 'part of the predicate' is taken literally, a simple negative also has a negation particle that could be considered part of the predicate. The response is that the particle in a simple negative acts on the whole relation, not as a component of the predicate.
This step sharpens your understanding of what 'part of the predicate' means.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقسيم الحملية إلى معدولة ومحصلة وبسيطة وَقَدْ يُجْعَلُ حَرْفُ السَّلْبِ كلفظةِ : «لا»، و«غير»، و«ليس» جُزءاً مِنْ جُزْءٍ. الدسوقي: (قَوْلُهُ : وَقَدْ يُجْعَلُ حَرْفُ السَّلْبِ) أي: أداتُهُ الدَّالَّه عليه؛ كانت لا، أو غير، أو ليس. (قَوْلُهُ : وَغَيْر و«ليس) فيه : أنَّ «غير» اسم، و«ليس» فعل، فلا يصح أنْ يكونا مثالين لحرفِ السَّلْبِ، إلا أن يُقال مثل بذلك إشارة إلى أنَّ مُرادَ المصنفِ مِثَالَيْنِ بحرفِ السَّلْبِ؛ لفظُهُ وَمَا يَدلُّ عليه . العطار: (قَوْلُهُ: جُزْءاً مِنْ جُزْءٍ) شملَ كلامُهُ السَّالبة، فالتَّعريفُ غيرُ مانع، ويُجابُ : بأنَّ حرف النَّفي في السَّالبة قاطع للنسبة وليس جزءاً منها.
Division of the categorical proposition into modified, simple, and simple? [Clearer: positive and simple?] And the negation particle like 'la', 'ghayr', and 'laysa' may be made part of the predicate. Dassuqi: His statement 'and the negation particle' meaning its indicator that indicates it, whether it is 'la', or 'ghayr', or 'laysa'. His statement 'and 'ghayr' and 'laysa' contains the objection that 'ghayr' is a noun and 'laysa' is a verb, so they cannot be examples of a negation particle, unless it is said that he gave examples by that to indicate that what the author intends is two examples of the negation particle: its word and what it indicates. Attar: His statement 'part of a part' includes the negative proposition in his speech, so the definition is not exclusive, and the answer is that the negation particle in the negative proposition cuts off the relation and is not part of it.
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This passage defines the modified proposition (al-ma'dula) and clarifies the role of the negation particle. The heading states that categorical propositions are divided into three types: modified (ma'dula), simple (muhassala), and simple (basita)? The matn asserts that the negation particle, such as 'la', 'ghayr', or 'laysa', can be made part of the predicate. Dassuqi explains that 'ḥarf al-salb' means the tool indicating negation, whether it is a particle, a noun, or a verb. He then raises an objection: 'ghayr' is a noun and 'laysa' is a verb, so they cannot be called 'ḥarf' (particle). The answer given is that the author used them as examples to show that he means both the word and its meaning. Attar then points out that if the negation particle is made part of the predicate, the definition would also include simple negative propositions (al-saliba), since they too contain a negation particle. This would make the definition not exclusive. The response is that in a simple negative proposition, the negation particle cuts off the relation (qati' li al-nisba) and is not part of the predicate, whereas in a modified proposition, the particle is integrated into the predicate. This distinction is crucial for understanding the classification of categorical propositions.

Key Takeaway
A modified proposition integrates the negation particle into the predicate, whereas a simple negative uses the particle to cut the relation; this distinction preserves the exclusivity of the definition.

What Counts as a Negation Particle? The Debate on 'Ghayr' and 'Laysa'
Discusses the scope of 'ḥarf al-salb' and the objection against including non-particles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Dassuqi's objection to including nouns and verbs as examples of 'ḥarf al-salb' and explain the resolution offered by the commentator.

Core Idea
Not every negation word is a particle; the author stretches 'ḥarf' to include nouns and verbs by meaning.

The author lists 'ghayr' and 'laysa' as examples of negation particles, but Dassuqi objects that they are not particles at all—'ghayr' is a noun and 'laysa' is a verb. This lesson unpacks the grammatical objection and the logical resolution that broadens the term 'ḥarf' to include any tool of negation, regardless of part of speech.

Guided Path
1
Find the examples of negation particles given by the author
In the matn, the author says 'the negation particle like la, ghayr, and laysa may be made part of the predicate'. Extract these three examples.
'La' is a particle, but 'ghayr' is a noun and 'laysa' is a verb grammatically.
2
Recognize Dassuqi's grammatical objection
Dassuqi points out that 'ghayr' is an ism (noun) and 'laysa' is a fi'l (verb), so they cannot be correct examples of a 'ḥarf' (particle). This is a linguistic critique.
The objection holds if 'ḥarf' is taken in its strict grammatical sense.
3
Understand the intended meaning of 'ḥarf al-salb'
Dassuqi then suggests that the author used these examples to indicate that 'ḥarf al-salb' refers to both the word and what it indicates—i.e., any tool of negation, not just grammatical particles.
This broadens the term to include nouns and verbs that function as negation tools.
4
Evaluate the resolution
Consider whether the resolution is satisfactory. The author's intent is logical, not grammatical; the category 'ḥarf al-salb' is defined by function, not part of speech. This resolves the objection.
This step shows how logical terminology can override strict grammatical categories.

Why Modification Does Not Apply to Conditionals
Author argues modification does not apply to conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why modification ('adl) does not apply to conditional propositions, based on the argument that conditionals judge the connection between relations, not the terms.

Core Idea
Modification is about terms; conditionals are about connections.

In logic, modification refers to incorporating negation into the predicate or subject term. But does this apply to conditional statements? The commentators al-Dasuqi and al-Attar discuss this, with al-Attar arguing that in conditionals, the judgment is about the relationship between two propositions—entailment, contrariety, or agreement—not about the individual terms. Therefore, modification is irrelevant here. Let's trace their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Recall what modification means in categorical propositions
Modification occurs when the negation is part of the term, e.g., 'non-seeing'. In categorical propositions, modification alters the predicate or subject term itself.
This is the standard definition in Aristotelian logic.
2
Identify the structure of a conditional proposition
A conditional (shartiyya) links two relations (nisbatayn), not two terms. It judges the connection (ittisal) or disjunction (infisal) between them.
For example, 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists' links two relations.
3
Analyze the examples al-Dasuqi gives
Al-Dasuqi provides examples like 'If the sun is not rising, then night exists'. At first glance, these seem like modified antecedents/consequents, but the negation is actually part of the relation, not a modification of a term.
The negation in 'the sun is not rising' is a negated relation, not a term modification.
4
Understand al-Attar's counterargument
Al-Attar states that in conditionals, the judgment is about the connection or disconnection between the two relations, regardless of whether the relations are affirmative, negative, or modified. Thus, modification is not a property of the conditional itself.
This means the quality of the conditional is determined by the connection (luzum, 'inad, ittifaq), not by the terms.
5
Conclude that modification does not apply to conditionals
Since conditionals are about connections and modification is about terms, applying modification to the conditional as a whole is a category mistake. The notion of a 'modified conditional' is not logically sound.
This follows from the nature of conditionals as relational judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العطار المعدولةِ ; المعدولةُ بها بناءً على الحذفِ والإيصال والاستتارِ كَمَا في لفظ مُشترك، أو لأنَّ الأصل في التَّعبيرِ عَن الأطرافِ هو الأمورُ الثَّبوتيَّةُ ; لأنَّ الوجود هو السَّابِقُ، والسَّالبُ مُضافٌ إليهِ، فَفي التَّعبيرِ عَن طَرفَي القضيَّةِ بالسَّلْبِ: عدولٌ عَن الأصل . وقد لا يكون حرفُ السَّلْبِ جزءاً لا من المحمول ولا من الموضوع. الدسوقي ومثالُ الشَّرطيَّةِ المعدولة المقدَّم : (إنْ لم تكن الشَّمس طالعة ; كانَ اللَّيلُ موجوداً) . ومثالها معدولةُ التَّالي : (إنْ كانَتِ الشَّمس طالعةٌ ; لم يكن اللَّيلُ موجوداً ) . ومثالها معدولةُ المقدَّمِ والتَّالي: (إنْ لم تكنِ الشَّمس طالعة; لم يكنِ النَّهارُ موجوداً). العطار فيها بالاتِّصالِ بينَ النِّسبتَيْنِ أو الانفصال أو سلبهما ; سواء كانَ النِّسبتانَ مُوجِبَتَيْنِ أو سالبتين أو معدولتينِ، وَكَذا الجهةُ ; إذ اللُّزوم والعناد والاتِّفاقُ أقسامُ الحُكْم الشَّرطيّ لا كيفيَّتُهُ، وَكَذَا الحقيقيَّةُ والخارجيَّةُ ; إذ الحُكْمُ في كُلِّ شَرطيَّةٍ شامل لجميعِ التَّقاديرِ الممكنة، ولا يقتصر على التّقادير المحققة ا.هـ. واعلَمْ أَنَّ الفرق بينَ السَّالبةِ البسيطة والموجبة المعدولة المحمول ; أما بحسب المعنى فهوَ أَنَّ الحُكْمَ في الأُولى بانتزاع المحمول عن الموضوع ، وفي الثانية : الحكم بثبوتِ عدمِ المحمول للموضوع، فَالسَّالبه أعم بحسب المادَّةِ، فإنَّ صدقها لا يتوقَّفُ على وجودِ الموضوع، بخلاف الموجبة وإِنْ كانَتْ مَعدولةٌ ; فإنَّ الشَّيء ما لم يثبت; لا يثبت له اللَّفْظِ ; فإنْ كانَتِ العبارة فارسيَّةً ; فالأمر ظاهر; لأنَّ لغةَ الفُرْسِ.
al-Attar: The modified [proposition] is modified based on deletion, connection, and concealment like in a homonym, or because the original in expressing the terms is affirmative matters, because existence is prior, and negation is additional to it. So in expressing the two terms of the proposition with negation, it is a deviation from the original. And it is possible that the negation particle is not part of either the predicate or the subject. al-Dasuqi: An example of a conditional with modified antecedent: 'If the sun is not rising, then night exists'. And an example with modified consequent: 'If the sun is rising, then night does not exist'. And an example with both antecedent and consequent modified: 'If the sun is not rising, then daytime does not exist'. al-Attar: In it [the conditional] is the connection between the two relations or disjunction or their negation, whether the two relations are affirmative, negative, or modified. Likewise the aspect; for entailment, contrariety, and agreement are species of conditional judgment, not its quality. Likewise the actual and external [judgments], for the judgment in every conditional is inclusive of all possible circumstances, not restricted to actual circumstances. End. And know that the difference between the simple negative and the affirmative with modified predicate: in terms of meaning, it is that the judgment in the first is the removal of the predicate from the subject, and in the second it is the judgment of the affirmation of the non-existence of the predicate for the subject. So the simple negative is more general in terms of matter, for its truth does not depend on the existence of the subject, unlike the affirmative even if it is modified, because a thing cannot be affirmed for something that is not established. If the expression is Persian, the matter is clear because the Persian language...
Source pages p. 526

Commentary
This passage first discusses the concept of modification ('adl) in general, stating that modification is a deviation from the original way of expressing terms, which is affirmative. It then notes that it is possible for the negation particle not to be part of either term. Then al-Dasuqi provides examples of conditional propositions (shartiyya) with modified parts: modified antecedent, modified consequent, and both. But al-Attar counters that in conditionals, the judgment is about the connection (ittisal) or disjunction (infisal) or their negation, regardless of whether the component relations are affirmative, negative, or modified. Therefore modification does not truly apply to conditionals because the negation relates to the connection, not to the terms. The passage ends with a distinction between a simple negative proposition (saliba basita) and an affirmative with modified predicate (mujaba ma'dulat al-mahmul). The simple negative simply negates the predicate from the subject (e.g., 'Zayd is not seeing'), while the modified affirmative affirms the negation of the predicate (e.g., 'Zayd is non-seeing'). The simple negative is more general in matter because it can be true even if the subject does not exist, whereas the modified affirmative requires the subject to exist for the predicate to be affirmed. The comment about Persian language hints that in Persian, the distinction is clearer because the negative particle is attached to the predicate word.

العدول في الشرطيات
الاتصال
الانفصال

A) Because conditionals are always affirmative.

D) Because conditionals do not have predicates.

Key Takeaway
Modification is a property of categorical proposition terms; conditionals are about the connection between relations, so 'modified conditionals' are a misnomer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the conditional statement: 'If the servant obeys, then God is pleased.' Explain why this conditional cannot be described as having a 'modified antecedent' or 'modified consequent' according to the commentators' argument.
?
Construct an argument that modification applies only to categorical propositions, using the distinction between term modification and connection quality.

Simple Negative vs. Modified Affirmative
Author distinguishes simple negative from modified affirmative

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a simple negative proposition (saliba basita) and an affirmative with modified predicate (mujaba ma'dulat al-mahmul) in terms of meaning and existential presupposition.

Core Idea
Simple negative does not require subject existence; modified affirmative does.

Two types of propositions can appear similar: 'Zayd is not seeing' (simple negative) and 'Zayd is non-seeing' (modified affirmative). But they are not equivalent. The simple negative merely denies that seeing holds for Zayd, and is true even if Zayd does not exist. The modified affirmative affirms the property of non-seeing, which presupposes Zayd exists. Let's understand the logical distinction as presented in the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two propositions in Arabic
The text contrasts al-saliba al-basita (simple negative) and al-mujaba al-ma'dulat al-mahmul (affirmative with modified predicate). Recognize the difference: the first has no negation in the predicate term; the second has a negated concept as predicate.
For example, 'Zayd is not seeing' vs. 'Zayd is non-seeing'.
2
Compare their meaning (bi hasab al-ma'na)
In the simple negative, the judgment is the removal (intiza') of the predicate from the subject. In the modified affirmative, the judgment is the affirmation of the non-existence (thubut 'adam) of the predicate for the subject.
The former negates the predicate; the latter affirms a negative property.
3
Check the condition for truth regarding subject existence
The simple negative does not require the subject to exist; it is true if the subject does not exist (since negation of existence is true). The modified affirmative requires the subject to exist because you are affirming a property (even if negative) about it.
If Zayd does not exist, 'Zayd is not seeing' is true, but 'Zayd is non-seeing' is false (or meaningless) because there is no subject to have the property.
4
Generalize the difference in scope
The simple negative is more general (a'amm) in matter: it is true in both cases (subject exists and predicate absent, or subject nonexistent). The modified affirmative is only true when the subject exists and the predicate is absent.
This is why the text says 'fa-al-saliba a'amm bi hasab al-madda'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العطار المعدولةِ ; المعدولةُ بها بناءً على الحذفِ والإيصال والاستتارِ كَمَا في لفظ مُشترك، أو لأنَّ الأصل في التَّعبيرِ عَن الأطرافِ هو الأمورُ الثَّبوتيَّةُ ; لأنَّ الوجود هو السَّابِقُ، والسَّالبُ مُضافٌ إليهِ، فَفي التَّعبيرِ عَن طَرفَي القضيَّةِ بالسَّلْبِ: عدولٌ عَن الأصل . وقد لا يكون حرفُ السَّلْبِ جزءاً لا من المحمول ولا من الموضوع. الدسوقي ومثالُ الشَّرطيَّةِ المعدولة المقدَّم : (إنْ لم تكن الشَّمس طالعة ; كانَ اللَّيلُ موجوداً) . ومثالها معدولةُ التَّالي : (إنْ كانَتِ الشَّمس طالعةٌ ; لم يكن اللَّيلُ موجوداً ) . ومثالها معدولةُ المقدَّمِ والتَّالي: (إنْ لم تكنِ الشَّمس طالعة; لم يكنِ النَّهارُ موجوداً). العطار فيها بالاتِّصالِ بينَ النِّسبتَيْنِ أو الانفصال أو سلبهما ; سواء كانَ النِّسبتانَ مُوجِبَتَيْنِ أو سالبتين أو معدولتينِ، وَكَذا الجهةُ ; إذ اللُّزوم والعناد والاتِّفاقُ أقسامُ الحُكْم الشَّرطيّ لا كيفيَّتُهُ، وَكَذَا الحقيقيَّةُ والخارجيَّةُ ; إذ الحُكْمُ في كُلِّ شَرطيَّةٍ شامل لجميعِ التَّقاديرِ الممكنة، ولا يقتصر على التّقادير المحققة ا.هـ. واعلَمْ أَنَّ الفرق بينَ السَّالبةِ البسيطة والموجبة المعدولة المحمول ; أما بحسب المعنى فهوَ أَنَّ الحُكْمَ في الأُولى بانتزاع المحمول عن الموضوع ، وفي الثانية : الحكم بثبوتِ عدمِ المحمول للموضوع، فَالسَّالبه أعم بحسب المادَّةِ، فإنَّ صدقها لا يتوقَّفُ على وجودِ الموضوع، بخلاف الموجبة وإِنْ كانَتْ مَعدولةٌ ; فإنَّ الشَّيء ما لم يثبت; لا يثبت له اللَّفْظِ ; فإنْ كانَتِ العبارة فارسيَّةً ; فالأمر ظاهر; لأنَّ لغةَ الفُرْسِ.
al-Attar: The modified [proposition] is modified based on deletion, connection, and concealment like in a homonym, or because the original in expressing the terms is affirmative matters, because existence is prior, and negation is additional to it. So in expressing the two terms of the proposition with negation, it is a deviation from the original. And it is possible that the negation particle is not part of either the predicate or the subject. al-Dasuqi: An example of a conditional with modified antecedent: 'If the sun is not rising, then night exists'. And an example with modified consequent: 'If the sun is rising, then night does not exist'. And an example with both antecedent and consequent modified: 'If the sun is not rising, then daytime does not exist'. al-Attar: In it [the conditional] is the connection between the two relations or disjunction or their negation, whether the two relations are affirmative, negative, or modified. Likewise the aspect; for entailment, contrariety, and agreement are species of conditional judgment, not its quality. Likewise the actual and external [judgments], for the judgment in every conditional is inclusive of all possible circumstances, not restricted to actual circumstances. End. And know that the difference between the simple negative and the affirmative with modified predicate: in terms of meaning, it is that the judgment in the first is the removal of the predicate from the subject, and in the second it is the judgment of the affirmation of the non-existence of the predicate for the subject. So the simple negative is more general in terms of matter, for its truth does not depend on the existence of the subject, unlike the affirmative even if it is modified, because a thing cannot be affirmed for something that is not established. If the expression is Persian, the matter is clear because the Persian language...
Source pages p. 526

Commentary
This passage first discusses the concept of modification ('adl) in general, stating that modification is a deviation from the original way of expressing terms, which is affirmative. It then notes that it is possible for the negation particle not to be part of either term. Then al-Dasuqi provides examples of conditional propositions (shartiyya) with modified parts: modified antecedent, modified consequent, and both. But al-Attar counters that in conditionals, the judgment is about the connection (ittisal) or disjunction (infisal) or their negation, regardless of whether the component relations are affirmative, negative, or modified. Therefore modification does not truly apply to conditionals because the negation relates to the connection, not to the terms. The passage ends with a distinction between a simple negative proposition (saliba basita) and an affirmative with modified predicate (mujaba ma'dulat al-mahmul). The simple negative simply negates the predicate from the subject (e.g., 'Zayd is not seeing'), while the modified affirmative affirms the negation of the predicate (e.g., 'Zayd is non-seeing'). The simple negative is more general in matter because it can be true even if the subject does not exist, whereas the modified affirmative requires the subject to exist for the predicate to be affirmed. The comment about Persian language hints that in Persian, the distinction is clearer because the negative particle is attached to the predicate word.

Key Terms
السالبة البسيطة
الموجبة المعدولة المحمول

C) The simple negative does not require Zayd to exist, while the modified affirmative does.

Key Takeaway
The simple negative is more general in matter because its truth does not depend on the subject's existence, unlike the modified affirmative which does.

Two Meanings of General Conditional and Temporal Necessity
Author explains two interpretations of general conditional and introduces temporal necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two interpretations of general conditional (al-mashruta al-‘amma) and identify the absolute temporal (al-waqtiyya al-mutlaqa).

Core Idea
General conditional has two meanings: one with the description as a condition, the other as mere time marker.

Precision in conditional propositions is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic logic. This passage from the Tadhheeb commentary distinguishes two ways to understand 'general conditional' and introduces the absolute temporal. By parsing these definitions, you will sharpen your ability to classify modal propositions correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings of mashruta
Find where the commentator explicitly states the two possible interpretations. The first meaning requires the predicate to be necessary as long as the subject has a specific description, with the description as a condition. The second meaning merely requires the predicate to hold whenever the description exists, without the description being a condition.
The second meaning is the technical usage according to later commentators.
2
Extract the examples for each meaning
Notice the examples: 'Every eclipsed thing is dark at the time of obstruction' is used for overlap; 'Every human is an animal' is absolute necessity. The commentator also gives 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer' as an example of the conditional.
These examples clarify which propositions fall under which meaning.
3
Understand the relationship between meanings
The passage explains that the two meanings of mashruta have a 'general and specific from one aspect' relationship in terms of actuality, not concept. Also, between absolute necessity and general conditional (first meaning) there is generality and specificity from one aspect.
This relationship is based on actual truth values, not conceptual containment.
4
Define the absolute temporal
Find the definition: a proposition where the predicate holds for the subject at a specific time, not necessarily due to a description. The temporal is more general than the conditional (second meaning) because every conditional is temporal but not vice versa.
The example 'Every eclipsed thing is dark at the time of obstruction' is suitable for both conditional (second meaning) and temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(قَوْلُهُ: كلّ منخسف ... إلخ) هذا مثال لما تنفرد به المشروطة، فإن الإظلام ليس ضرورياً لذات القمر ما دام ذاته حتى تكون ضرورية، وإنما هو ضروري له بشرط الانخساف فهو بمثابة : كلّ كاتب متحرك الأصابع بالضرورة ما دام كاتباً . (قَوْلُهُ : كلّ إنسان ... إلخ) المناسب أن يمثل بكل كاتب حيوان بالضرورة ما دام الذات مما لم يكن لوصف الموضوع دخل في ضرورة النسبة، وأما المثال الذي ذكره؛ فهو صورة الاجتماع كما هو منصوص . واعلَمْ أَنَّ مَا ذكَرَه المصنِّفُ في تعريف المشروطة يحتمل كلا المعنيين ؛ لأنَّ قولَه : ما دامَ وصفه يحتمل أن يراد به بشرط الوصف، فتكون مشروطة بالمعنى الأوّل، ويحتمل أن يراد به ما دام الوصفُ بلا اعتبار الاشتراط، فتكون مشروطة بالمعنى الثاني. [الوقتيَّةُ المطلقة]: الدسوقي (أَوْ فِي وَقْتٍ مُعَيَّنٍ) عطفٌ على قوله : «مَا دَامَ ذَاتُ الموضُوعِ»؛ أي: والمشروطة العامة في : كلّ كاتب مُتحرّكُ الأصابع، وبينهما وبين المشروطة العامة بالمعنى الثَّاني عمومٌ مُطلقٌ ، والضَّروريَّةُ أخص، فكلُّ مُنخسفٍ مُظلم وقتَ الحيلولة؛ يصلح مثالاً لهما وتنفرد المشروطة في : كلّ ناطق إنسان. وأمَّا بين المشروطة بالمعنى الأوّلِ والثَّاني ؛ فَالعمومُ والخصوص الوجهيُّ كَمَا هو بَيِّنْ مِنَ الشَّارح. (قَوْلُهُ : بِلَا اعْتِبَارِ الِاشْتِرَاطِ) أي : وحينئذٍ ؛ فَتسميتُهَا مَشروطةٌ تَسمية اصطلاحية لَا مُناسبةً فيها . العطار تتحقَّقُ باعتبار أوقاتِ الكتابةِ مَشروطةً بكونها وقتَ الكتابة، لكن المشروطة بهذا المعنى لم تعتبر بينهم، بلْ كُلَّما تصدقُ الضَّرورة مشروطة بكونها في جميع أوقاتِ الوصفِ؛ تُقيَّدُ بكونها في جميع أوقاتِ الوصف من غيرِ اشتراط أن يكون وقت الوصفِ، بل يُضافُ الوقتُ إلى الوصفِ لمجرَّدِ التَّعيين. (قَوْلُ الشَّارح : واعلم ... إلخ) قد علمت ما فيه وأنه يتعين حمل كلام المصنف على المعنى الثَّاني، ويجعل المعنى الأوّل مقابلاً له تطلق عليه المشروطة العامة أيضاً بطريق الاشتراك اللفظي . (قَوْلُهُ : يصلح ... إلخ) لا يصلح إلا للمشروطة كما بينا، وقوله: وتنفرد... إلخ، بل يجتمعان فيه . ا.هـ. الشرنوبي.
Section: On the definition and its divisions. ...necessary for the essence of the subject at some time, so movement consequent to writing is not necessary for the essence of the subject absolutely; thus the conditional (mashruta) is true in the first meaning but not the second. al-Dasuqi: His saying 'absolutely' means at some time. Know that between absolute necessity and general conditional in the first meaning there is generality and specificity from one aspect, in terms of actuality not concept, because they are mutually exclusive; they meet in 'Every eclipsed thing is dark at the time of obstruction', and absolute necessity is unique to 'Every human is an animal'. al-Attar: 'for the essence of the subject' means its individuals at some time, so movement consequent to writing is not necessary; therefore he said 'since the description...' etc.; thus on this estimation he made the description a part; so the intended meaning of 'writer' is particular individuals without making the description, which is the source of necessity, a condition in it, and it is known that writing is not necessary for these individuals. By this you know that there is no basis for the saying of al-Muhanni: 'If this proof were sound, it would prove the falsity of intending the first meaning because it applies to it exactly' etc. And his saying 'for the essence of the subject' means the individuals as such, so it does not negate the necessity of its being established for some individuals due to tremor, for example. His saying 'absolutely': al-‘Isam said it is a generalization of necessity, not a restriction of the negated necessity to absoluteness, so that it would follow that negating absolute necessity does not necessitate other than necessity with the condition of the description, because of the possibility of specification at the time of the description. Then he said: There is a discussion here: just as necessity is realized with respect to the essence conditioned by writing; for negation... His saying 'Every eclipsed thing...' etc. is an example of what the conditional (mashruta) is unique to; for darkness is not necessary for the essence of the moon as long as its essence exists so that it would be necessary; rather it is necessary for it under the condition of eclipse, so it is like 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer'. His saying 'Every human...' etc.: the appropriate example is 'Every writer is an animal necessarily as long as the essence does not have the description of the subject entering into the necessity of the relation'; as for the example he mentioned, it is a case of commonality as stated. Know that what the author mentioned in the definition of the conditional (mashruta) admits both meanings; for his saying 'as long as its description' can be intended to mean with the condition of the description, so it is conditional in the first meaning, and can be intended to mean as long as the description exists without considering the condition, so it is conditional in the second meaning. [The absolute temporal (al-waqtiyya al-mutlaqa)]: al-Dasuqi: 'Or at a specific time' is a conjunction to his saying 'as long as the essence of the subject'; i.e., the general conditional in 'Every writer moves his fingers'; between it and the general conditional in the second meaning there is absolute generality, and the necessary is more specific; so 'Every eclipsed thing is dark at the time of obstruction' is suitable as an example for both, and the conditional is unique to 'Every rational being is a human'. As for between the conditional in the first and second meanings, there is generality and specificity from one aspect as is clear from the commentator. His saying 'without considering the condition': i.e., then it is called 'conditional' as a technical term with no appropriateness. al-Attar: It is realized with respect to the times of writing conditioned by its being the time of writing, but the conditional in this sense is not considered among them; rather whenever necessity is true conditioned by its being in all times of the description, it is restricted to being in all times of the description without the condition that it be the time of the description; rather time is added to the description merely for specification. (The commentator's saying 'Know... etc.') You have known what is in it and that the author's statement must be interpreted as the second meaning, and the first meaning is made parallel to it, also called general conditional by way of homonymy. (His saying 'is suitable... etc.') It is only suitable for the conditional as we showed; and his saying 'and it is unique... etc.'; rather they meet in it. End. al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 553

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the distinctions between different types of necessary propositions: absolute necessary, general conditional, and temporal. The passage opens with a discussion of the two possible meanings of 'general conditional' (al-mashruta al-‘amma). The first meaning requires that the predicate hold necessarily as long as the subject has a certain description, but the description itself is part of the condition. The second meaning merely requires that the predicate hold whenever the description exists, without the description being a condition for the necessity. The commentators (al-Dasuqi and al-Attar) then explain examples: 'Every eclipsed thing is dark at the time of obstruction' is an example where both meanings overlap; 'Every human is an animal' is an example of absolute necessity not conditional. The passage then introduces 'absolute temporal' (al-waqtiyya al-mutlaqa), which is a proposition where the predicate holds at a specific time, but not necessarily due to a description. The temporal is compared to the conditional: it is more general in some respects. The commentators clarify that the author's definition of the conditional admits both interpretations, and the correct one is the second (without condition), while the first is a homonymous usage. The passage ends with a note that the example 'Every eclipsed thing is dark at the time of obstruction' is suitable only for the conditional, not the temporal.

Key Terms
المشروطة العامة
الضرورية المطلقة
الوقتية المطلقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, what is the difference between the two meanings of 'general conditional' (al-mashruta al-‘amma)?
A) The first meaning requires the description to be part of the subject's essence; the second requires it to be an accident.
B) The first meaning requires the predicate to be necessary as long as the description exists, with the description as a condition; the second requires the predicate to hold whenever the description exists, without the description being a condition.
C) The first meaning applies only to universal propositions; the second applies only to particular propositions.
D) The first meaning is used in logic; the second is used in grammar.

Key Takeaway
General conditional propositions must be parsed carefully to determine whether the description is a condition for the necessity or merely a temporal marker.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which meaning of general conditional (first or second) is intended in the fiqh maxim: 'Every sale is valid as long as mutual consent exists.' Explain your reasoning.
?

The Real Disconnected Conditional: Definition and Key Features
Author defines the disconnected conditional and its real subtype with examples and commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a real disconnected conditional (al-munfasila al-haqiqiyya) by verifying that its two parts mutually exclude each other in both truth and falsehood.

Core Idea
A real disconnected conditional requires complete mutual exclusion: both truth and falsehood cannot be shared.

In this passage, the author defines the disconnected conditional (al-munfasila) as a proposition that judges mutual exclusion (tanafi) between two relations. He then specifies the 'real' subtype (al-haqiqiyya) as one that judges mutual exclusion in both truth and falsehood simultaneously. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing disjunctive arguments in Islamic reasoning, where the logical relationship between alternatives must be precisely determined. The commentary by al-Dasuqi and al-Attar further clarifies the meaning of the terms and the logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two main types of conditionals
The author states that the conditional is either connected (muttasila) or disconnected (munfasila). Connected conditionals were defined earlier as affirming or negating a relation under a condition. Disconnected conditionals involve mutual exclusion between two relations.
2
Extract the definition of a disconnected conditional
The passage says: '...it is disconnected if it is judged in it by the mutual exclusion of two relations, or by the non-mutual exclusion of them in truth and falsehood.' Note the two subcases: one with mutual exclusion, one without. The 'real' type is the first.
The negative form (non-mutual exclusion) is the subject of a different subtype.
3
Identify the real disconnected conditional (haqiqiyya)
The real disconnected conditional is defined as the one that judges mutual exclusion in both truth and falsehood together. This means its two parts cannot be simultaneously true nor simultaneously false. For example, in Kalam, the disjunction 'The Qur'an is either created or uncreated' is a real disconnected conditional because the two possibilities are mutually exclusive and collectively exhaustive (according to the theological framework).
The definition explicitly requires both truth and falsehood mutual exclusion, not just one.
4
Distinguish the real from other types
The passage contrasts the real (haqiqiyya) with its negative counterpart (where mutual exclusion is absent). In Islamic logic, the real disconnected conditional is also known as maniat al-jam' wal-khuluw (preventing conjunction and disjunction). Understanding this distinction helps in analyzing disjunctive syllogisms.
Other subtypes like maniat al-jam' only exclude conjunction, not disjunction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسامُ الشَّرطيَّةِ الْمُنْفَصِلة (وَمُنْفَصِلَةٌ) بالرفع؛ عطفٌ على قوله : «متصلة»؛ أي: الشَّرطيَّة إِمَّا متّصلة إن حكم فيها بثبوت نسبةٍ أو نفيها على تقدير أخرى كما مرَّ. وإِمَّا منفصلةٌ إنْ حُكِمَ فِيْهَا بِتَنَافِي نِسْبَتَيْنِ، أَوْ لَا تَنَافِيْهِمَا صِدْقًا وَكَذِبًا، وَهِيَ الحقيقية. فالمنفصلة الحقيقية : هي التي حُكِمَ فيها بتنافي نسبتين، أو عدمِ تنافيهما في الصدق والكذب معًا. قال الدسوقي: وقوله: (أو لا تنافيهما)؛ أي أو بعدم تنافيهما في الصدق والكذب، وهذا في السَّالبةِ، فقولُهُ: (صدقًا وكذبًا) راجع لكلِّ مِنَ الأمرين، والمراد بالصدق؛ الثبوتُ، وَبِالكذب؛ الارتفاع. قال العطار: (قَوْلُهُ : إِنْ حُكِمَ فِيْهَا لا يَخفَى أَنَّ المقسَّمَ مُلاحظ في الأقسام، فالمعنى قضيَّةٌ شَرطيَّةٌ مُحْكِمَ فيها ... إلخ.
Sections of the disconnected conditional: (And disconnected) in the nominative, as a conjunction to his statement: 'connected'; that is: the conditional is either connected if it is judged in it by affirming a relation or negating it upon another condition as previously mentioned. Or it is disconnected if it is judged in it by the mutual exclusion of two relations, or by the non-mutual exclusion of them in truth and falsehood, and it is the real. So the real disconnected is that which is judged in it by the mutual exclusion of two relations, or by the absence of their mutual exclusion in truth and falsehood together. Al-Dasuqi said: 'And his statement: (or non-mutual exclusion of them) i.e. or by the absence of their mutual exclusion in truth and falsehood, and this is in the negative. So his statement: 'in truth and falsehood' refers to each of the two matters, and by truth is meant affirmation, and by falsehood is meant negation.' Al-Attar said: 'His statement: if it is judged in it, it is not hidden that the divided is considered in the divisions, so the meaning is a conditional proposition judged in it... etc.'
Source pages p. 608

Commentary
This passage defines the disconnected conditional proposition (al-munfasila) and its real subtype (al-haqiqiyya). It begins by stating that the conditional proposition is either connected (muttasila) — already defined earlier — or disconnected. The disconnected conditional is defined as a proposition in which there is a judgment of mutual exclusion (tanafi) between two relations (i.e., the two component propositions). The definition includes two cases: either the judgment is that they mutually exclude each other in truth and falsehood simultaneously, or that they do not mutually exclude each other in both respects. The latter case (non-mutual exclusion) is the negative form of the disconnected conditional. The text then specifies that the 'real' disconnected conditional (al-haqiqiyya) is the one that judges mutual exclusion in truth and falsehood together. The commentator al-Dasuqi clarifies that the phrase 'or non-mutual exclusion of them' refers to the negative version, and that 'truth' means affirmation (thubut) and 'falsehood' means negation (irtifa'). Al-Attar then comments that the divided (the conditional) is considered in the divisions, so the meaning is that a conditional proposition is judged in it... (the commentary continues). The key point is that the real disconnected conditional is characterized by complete mutual exclusion: its two parts cannot both be true nor both be false. This is exemplified by propositions like 'This number is either even or odd' (since these two mutually exclusive and exhaustive). The passage also distinguishes this from other types of disconnected conditionals like maniat al-jam' (exclusive of conjunction) and maniat al-khuluw (exclusive of disjunction). The definition is foundational for understanding logical relations between propositions.

Key Terms
منفصلة (munfasila): A conditional proposition in which the judgment involves mutual exclusion (tanafi) between two relations (component propositions). [Note: In earlier logic, munfasila could also include disjunctive without mutual exclusion; here it is clearly defined by tanafi.]
حقيقية (haqiqiyya): The real disconnected conditional that judges mutual exclusion in both truth and falsehood between its two parts. [Note: Later texts tend to use haqiqiyya as the standard type of munfasila, but some variations exist in classification.]
تنافي (tanafi): Mutual exclusion; the relationship where two propositions cannot be simultaneously true nor simultaneously false. [Note: Stable term across the tradition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does a real disconnected conditional (al-munfasila al-haqiqiyya) judge?
A) Mutual exclusion in truth only
B) Mutual exclusion in falsehood only
C) Mutual exclusion in both truth and falsehood
D) Non-mutual exclusion in truth and falsehood

Key Takeaway
The real disconnected conditional is defined by complete mutual exclusion: its two parts cannot be both true nor both false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Ten Categories
intermediate
7 min
Divisions of Conditional Propositions
Author classifies conditional propositions into muttasila and munfasila, and further into similar or different parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify conditional propositions as muttasila or munfasila, and subdivide muttasila into six types based on the categorical or conditional nature of its antecedent and consequent.

Core Idea
The connected conditional has a natural direction; the disconnected does not.

In this lesson, we examine how Islamic logicians divide conditional propositions. The author distinguishes two main categories—connected (muttasila) and disconnected (munfasila)—and then shows that only the connected type admits further subdivision when its components differ. This classification is essential for analyzing compound arguments in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main types
Look at the opening sentence: the author says 'أَقْسَامُ الشَّرْطِيةِ' then contrasts 'مُتَّصِلَة' and 'مُنْفَصِلَة'. These are the two fundamental types of conditionals.
Muttasila corresponds to 'if...then', munfasila to 'either...or'.
2
Analyze the example given
The example: 'كلَّما كانَ دائماً إمَّا أن يكون العدد زوجاً أو فرداً...' This is a muttasila where the parts are themselves munfasila. Notice how the antecedent and consequent are full propositions.
This illustrates a compound conditional: a muttasila whose parts are munfasila.
3
List the possibilities when parts differ
The author says the two parts can be: (1) categorical and muttasila, (2) categorical and munfasila, (3) muttasila and munfasila. These are the three combinations of different types.
When both parts are of the same type (e.g., both categorical), it is the 'similar' case, which is not detailed here.
4
Understand why muttasila can be subdivided
In a muttasila, the antecedent is the entailing part (الملزوم) and the consequent is the entailed (اللازم). This gives a natural direction, so we can swap the order: antecedent categorical and consequent conditional, or vice versa.
This directionality is absent in munfasila because its two sides are symmetric.
5
Calculate the total divisions
For each of the three combinations of different parts, there are two arrangements (categorical first or conditional first). Thus 3 × 2 = 6 divisions for muttasila. Munfasila has no such subdivision.
The author explicitly states: 'فالمتَّصلة مِنَ المختلفتين ستَّةُ أقسام'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي أَقْسَامِ الشَّرْطِيةِ أَوْ مُنْفَصِلَتَانِ) كَقولِنَا : كلَّما كانَ دائماً إمَّا أن يكون العدد زوجاً أو فرداً ، فدائماً إما أن يكون منقسماً بمتساويين أو غير منقسم وإمَّا أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً أو فرداً وإما ألا يكون هذا العدد لا زوجاً أو لا فرداً. (أَوْ مُخْتَلِفَتَانِ) في الحمل والاتصال والانفصال، بأن يكونَ طَرَفاها، إمَّا حمليَّةٌ ومتّصلةٌ، أو حملية ومنفصلة، أو متصلة ومنفصلة، والأمثلة غير خافية على المتأمّل . ثمَّ إِنَّ هذهِ الأقسام الثَّلاثةَ تنقسم في المتَّصلة إلى قسمين ؛ لِمَا ثبتَ أَنَّ امتياز قسمَيْنِ؛ المقدَّم فيها عَنِ التَّالي بحسبِ الطَّبع ، فَالملزوم فيها مُتعيّن بأن يكونَ مُقدَّماً، واللازم تالياً، والاستلزامُ مِنَ الجانبين غير ضروري، والفرق بين مُقدَّمها حملية، وتاليها مُتَصلةٌ وَمُنفصلةٌ ، ومُقدَّمُها وتاليها بالعكس ،ظاهر، بخلافِ المنفصلة، فَلَا تنقسم فيها إليهما ؛ لعدم الامتياز على الوجه المذكور، فالمتّصلة مِنَ المختلفتين ستَّةُ أقسام.
Chapter: On the divisions of the conditional. Or two disjunctives) like our saying: Whenever it is permanent, either the number is even or odd, so permanently either it is divisible into two equals or not divisible, and either this number is even or odd, or it is not that this number is not even or not odd. (Or two differing) in categoricalness, connectedness, and disconnectedness, such that its two sides are either categorical and connected, or categorical and disconnected, or connected and disconnected, and examples are not hidden from one who contemplates. Then these three divisions are divided in the connected into two sections; because it is established that the distinction of two sections: the antecedent in it from the consequent according to nature, so the entailing in it is determined to be antecedent, and the consequent is consequent, and entailment from both sides is not necessary, and the difference between its antecedent being categorical and its consequent being connected or disconnected, and its antecedent and consequent being the opposite, is apparent, unlike the disconnected, so it is not divided into them; due to the lack of distinction in the mentioned manner. So the connected from the differing types has six divisions.
Source pages p. 629

Commentary
This passage defines and divides conditional propositions (al-shartiyya) in logic. The author first states that conditionals are of two main types: connected (muttasila, i.e., 'if...then') and disconnected (munfasila, i.e., 'either...or'). He then gives an example of a connected conditional composed of two disjunctives: 'Whenever it is permanent, either the number is even or odd, so permanently either it is divisible into two equals or not divisible.' This illustrates a compound conditional. Next, he discusses cases where the two parts of a conditional are different in kind: one may be categorical (hamliyya) and the other connected, or categorical and disconnected, or connected and disconnected. These are the three divisions when the parts are different (mukhtalifatani). The passage then focuses on the connected conditional (muttasila). Since in a connected conditional the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali) are naturally distinguished (the antecedent is the entailing part, the consequent the entailed), the muttasila can be divided into two types: those where the antecedent is categorical and the consequent is connected/disconnected, and the reverse. The disconnected conditional (munfasila), however, does not admit such a division because its two sides are not naturally distinguished as antecedent and consequent; they are mutually exclusive. Therefore, from the three different-part cases, the muttasila yields six divisions (three types of antecedent-consequent combinations times two directions). This classification is foundational for analyzing compound propositions in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
الشرطية
المتصلة
المنفصلة
الحملية
المقدم
التالي

A) Two

C) Six

Key Takeaway
The connected conditional (muttasila) can be subdivided based on the types of its parts, but the disconnected (munfasila) cannot because its sides are symmetric.
